the memoires of barry lyndon esq
chapter i my pedigree and family--undergo the influence of the tender passion since the day of adam there have be hardly a mischief do in this world but a woman have be at the bottom of it
ever since ours be a family and that must be very near adam s time so old noble and illustrious be the barrys as everybody know woman have play a mighty part with the destiny of our race
i presume that there be no gentleman in europe that have not hear of the house of barry of barryogue of the kingdom of ireland than which a more famous name be not to be find in gwillim or d hozier and though as a man of the world i have learn to despise heartily the claim of some pretender to high birth who have no more genealogy than the lacquey who clean my boot and though i laugh to utter scorn the boasting of many of my countryman who be all for descend from king of ireland and talk of a domain no big than would feed a pig as if it be a principality yet truth compel i to assert that my family be the noble of the island and perhaps of the universal world while their possession now insignificant and tear from we by war by treachery by the loss of time by ancestral extravagance by adhesion to the old faith and monarch be formerly prodigious and embrace many county at a time when ireland be vastly more prosperous than now
i would assume the irish crown over my coat-of-arm but that there be so many silly pretender to that distinction who bear it and render it common
who know but for the fault of a woman i might have be wear it now
you start with incredulity
i say why not
have there be a gallant chief to lead my countryman instead or pule knave who bent the knee to king richard ii they might have be freeman have there be a resolute leader to meet the murderous ruffian oliver cromwell we should have shake off the english for ever
but there be no barry in the field against the usurper on the contrary my ancestor simon de bary come over with the first-name monarch and marry the daughter of the then king of munster whose son in battle he pitilessly slay
in oliver s time it be too late for a chief of the name of barry to lift up his war-cry against that of the murderous brewer
we be prince of the land no long our unhappy race have lose its possession a century previously and by the most shameful treason
this i know to be the fact for my mother have often tell i the story and besides have work it in a worsted pedigree which hang up in the yellow saloon at barryville where we live
that very estate which the lyndon now possess in ireland be once the property of my race
rory barry of barryogue own it in elizabeth s time and half munster beside
the barry be always in feud with the o mahonys in those time and as it happen a certain english colonel pass through the former s country with a body of man-at-arm on the very day when the o mahonys have make an inroad upon our territory and carry off a frightful plunder of our flock and herd
this young englishman whose name be roger lyndon linden or lyndaine have be most hospitably receive by the barry and find he just on the point of carry an inroad into the o mahonys land offer the aid of himself and his lance and behave himself so well as it appear that the o mahonys be entirely overcome all the barrys property restore and with it say the old chronicle twice as much of the o mahonys good and cattle
it be the setting in of the winter season and the young soldier be press by the barry not to quit his house of barryogue and remain there during several month his man be quarter with barry s own gallowglasse man by man in the cottage round about
they conduct themselves as be their willnot with the most intolerable insolence towards the irish so much so that fight and murder continually ensue and the people vow to destroy they
the barry s son from whom i descend be as hostile to the english as any other man on his domain and as they would not go when bid he and his friend consult together and determine on destroy these english to a man
but they have let a woman into their plot and this be the barry s daughter
she be in love with the english lyndon and break the whole secret to he and the dastardly english prevent the just massacre of themselves by fall on the irish and destroy phaudrig barry my ancestor and many hundred of his man
the cross at barrycross near carrignadihioul be the spot where the odious butchery take place
lyndon marry the daughter of roderick barry and claim the estate which he leave and though the descendant of phaudrig be alive as indeed they be in my person footnote as we have never be able to find proof of the marriage of my ancestor phaudrig with his wife i make no doubt that lyndon destroy the contract and murder the priest and witness of the marriage b
l on appeal to the english court the estate be award to the englishman as have ever be the case where english and irish be concerned
thus have it not be for the weakness of a woman i should have be bear to the possession of those very estate which afterwards come to i by merit as you shall hear
but to proceed with my family history
my father be well known to the good circle in this kingdom as in that of ireland under the name of roaring harry barry
he be breed like many other young son of genteel family to the profession of the law be article to a celebrated attorney of sackville street in the city of dublin and from his great genius and aptitude for learning there be no doubt he would have make an eminent figure in his profession have not his social quality love of field-sport and extraordinary grace of manner mark he out for a high sphere
while he be attorney s clerk he keep seven race-horse and hunt regularly both with the kildare and wicklow hunt and ride on his grey horse endymion that famous match against captain punter which be still remember by lover of the sport and of which i cause a splendid picture to be make and hang over my dining-hall mantelpiece at castle lyndon
a year afterwards he have the honour of rid that very horse endymion before his late majesty king george ii
at new-market and win the plate there and the attention of the august sovereign
although he be only the second son of our family my dear father come naturally into the estate now miserably reduce to l400 a year for my grandfather s eld son cornelius barry call the chevalier borgne from a wound which he receive in germany remain constant to the old religion in which our family be educate and not only serve abroad with credit but against his most sacred majesty george ii
in the unhappy scotch disturbance in 45
we shall hear more of the chevalier hereafter
for the conversion of my father i have to thank my dear mother miss bell brady daughter of ulysses brady of castle brady county kerry esquire and j p
she be the most beautiful woman of her day in dublin and universally call the dasher there
see she at the assembly my father become passionately attach to she but her soul be above marry a papist or an attorney s clerk and so for the love of she the good old law be then in force my dear father slip into my uncle cornelius s shoe and take the family estate
besides the force of my mother s bright eye several person and of the genteelest society too contribute to this happy change and i have often hear my mother laughingly tell the story of my father s recantation which be solemnly pronounce at the tavern in the company of sir dick ringwood lord bagwig captain punter and two or three other young spark of the town
roar harry win 300 piece that very night at faro and lay the necessary information the next morning against his brother but his conversion cause a coolness between he and my uncle corney who join the rebel in consequence
this great difficulty be settle my lord bagwig lend my father his own yacht then lie at the pigeon house and the handsome bell brady be induce to run away with he to england although her parent be against the match and her lover as i have hear she tell many thousand of time be among the most numerous and the most wealthy in all the kingdom of ireland
they be marry at the savoy and my grandfather die very soon harry barry esquire take possession of his paternal property and support our illustrious name with credit in london
he pink the famous count tiercelin behind montague house he be a member of white s and a frequenter of all the chocolate-house and my mother likewise make no small figure
at length after his great day of triumph before his sacred majesty at newmarket harry s fortune be just on the point of be make for the gracious monarch promise to provide for he
but alas
he be take in charge by another monarch whose will have no delay or denial by death namely who seize upon my father at chester race leave i a helpless orphan
peace be to his ash
he be not faultless and dissipate all our princely family property but he be as brave a fellow as ever toss a bumper or call a main and he drive his coach-and- six like a man of fashion
i do not know whether his gracious majesty be much affect by this sudden demise of my father though my mother say he shed some royal tear on the occasion
but they help we to nothing and all that be find in the house for the wife and creditor be a purse of ninety guineas which my dear mother naturally take with the family plate and my father s wardrobe and her own and put they into our great coach drive off to holyhead whence she take shipping for ireland
my father s body accompany we in the fine hearse and plume money could buy for though the husband and wife have quarrel repeatedly in life yet at my father s death his high- spirited widow forget all her difference give he the grandest funeral that have be see for many a day and erect a monument over his remain for which i subsequently pay which declare he to be the wise pure and most affectionate of man
in perform these sad duty over her deceased lord the widow spend almost every guinea she have and indeed would have spend a great deal more have she discharge one-third of the demand which the ceremony occasion
but the people around our old house of barryogue although they do not like my father for his change of faith yet stand by he at this moment and be for exterminate the mute send by mr
plumer of london with the lamented remain
the monument and vault in the church be then alas
all that remain of my vast possession for my father have sell every stick of the property to one notley an attorney and we receive but a cold welcome in his house--a miserable old tumble-down place it be
footnote in another part of his memoir mr
barry will be find to describe this mansion as one of the most splendid palace in europe but this be a practice not unusual with his nation and with respect to the irish principality claim by he it be know that mr
barry s grandfather be an attorney and maker of his own fortune the splendour of the funeral do not fail to increase the widow barry s reputation as a woman of spirit and fashion and when she write to her brother michael brady that worthy gentleman immediately ride across the country to fle himself in her arm and to invite she in his wife s name to castle brady
mick and barry have quarrel as all man will and very high word have pass between they during barry s courtship of miss bell
when he take she off brady swear he would never forgive barry or bell but come to london in the year 46 he fall in once more with roaring harry and live in his fine house in clarges street and lose a few piece to he at play and break a watchman s head or two in his company all of which reminiscence endear bell and her son very much to the good-hearted gentleman and he receive we both with open arm
mrs
barry do not perhaps wisely at first make know to her friend what be her condition but arrive in a huge gilt coach with enormous armorial bearing be take by her sister- in-law and the rest of the county for a person of considerable property and distinction
for a time then and as be right and proper mrs
barry give the law at castle brady
she order the servant to and fro and teach they what indeed they much want a little london neatness and english redmond as i be call be treat like a little lord and have a maid and a footman to himself and honest mick pay their wages which be much more than he be use to do for his own domestics doing all in his power to make his sister decently comfortable under her affliction
mamma in return determine that when her affair be arrange she would make her kind brother a handsome allowance for her son s maintenance and her own and promise to have her handsome furniture bring over from clarges street to adorn the somewhat dilapidate room of castle brady
but it turn out that the rascally landlord seize upon every chair and table that ought by right to have belong to the widow
the estate to which i be heir be in the hand of rapacious creditor and the only mean of subsistence remain to the widow and child be a rent-charge of l50 upon my lord bagwig s property who have many turf-dealing with the deceased
and so my dear mother s liberal intention towards her brother be of course never fulfil
it must be confess very much to the discredit of mrs
brady of castle brady that when her sister-in-law s poverty be thus make manifest she forget all the respect which she have be accustom to pay she instantly turn my maid and man-servant out of door and tell mrs
barry that she might follow they as soon as she choose
mrs
mick be of a low family and a sordid way of thinking and after about a couple of year during which she have save almost all her little income the widow comply with madam brady s desire
at the same time give way to a just though prudently dissimulate resentment she make a vow that she would never enter the gate of castle brady while the lady of the house remain alive within they
she fit up her new abode with much economy and considerable taste and never for all her poverty abate a jot of the dignity which be her due and which all the neighbourhood award to she
how indeed could they refuse respect to a lady who have live in london frequent the most fashionable society there and have be present as she solemnly declare at court
these advantage give she a right which seem to be pretty unsparingly exercise in ireland by those native who have it the right of look down with scorn upon all person who have not have the opportunity of quit the mother-country and inhabit england for a while
thus whenever madam brady appear abroad in a new dress her sister-in-law would say poor creature
how can it be expect that she should know anything of the fashion and though please to be call the handsome widow as she be mrs
barry be still well pleased to be call the english widow
mrs
brady for her part be not slow to reply she use to say that the defunct barry be a bankrupt and a beggar and as for the fashionable society which he see he see it from my lord bagwig s side-table whose flatterer and hanger-on he be know to be
regard mrs
barry the lady of castle brady would make insinuation still more painful
however why should we allude to these charge or rake up private scandal of a hundred year old
it be in the reign of george ii that the above-name personage live and quarrel good or bad handsome or ugly rich or poor they be all equal now and do not the sunday paper and the court of law supply we every week with more novel and interesting slander
at any rate it must be allow that mrs
barry after her husband s death and her retirement live in such a way as to defy slander
for whereas bell brady have be the gay girl in the whole county of wexford with half the bachelor at her foot and plenty of smile and encouragement for every one of they bell barry adopt a dignified reserve that almost amount to pomposity and be as starch as any quakeress
many a man renew his offer to the widow who have be smite by the charm of the spinster but mrs
barry refuse all offer of marriage declare that she live now for her son only and for the memory of her depart saint
saint forsooth say ill-natured mrs
brady
harry barry be as big a sinner as ever be know and tis notorious that he and bell hat each other
if she willnot marry now depend on it the artful woman have a husband in her eye for all that and only wait until lord bagwig be a widower and suppose she do what then
be not the widow of a barry fit to marry with any lord of england
and be it not always say that a woman be to restore the fortune of the barry family
if my mother fancy that she be to be that woman i think it be a perfectly justifiable notion on her part for the earl my godfather be always most attentive to she i never know how deeply this notion of advance my interest in the world have take possession of mamma s mind until his lordship s marriage in the year 57 with miss goldmore the indian nabob s rich daughter
meanwhile we continue to reside at barryville and consider the smallness of our income keep up a wonderful state
of the half- dozen family that form the congregation at brady s town there be not a single person whose appearance be so respectable as that of the widow who though she always dress in mourning in memory of her deceased husband take care that her garment should be make so as to set off her handsome person to the great advantage and indeed i think spend six hour out of every day in the week in cutting trim and alter they to the fashion
she have the large of hoop and the handsome of furbelow and once a month under my lord bagwig s cover would come a letter from london contain the new account of the fashion there
her complexion be so brilliant that she have no call to use rouge as be the mode in those day
no she leave red and white she say and hence the reader may imagine how the two lady hat each other to madam brady whose yellow complexion no plaster could alter
in a word she be so accomplish a beauty that all the woman in the country take pattern by she and the young fellow from ten mile round would ride over to castle brady church to have the sight of she
but if like every other woman that ever i see or read of she be proud of her beauty to do her justice she be still more proud of her son and have say a thousand time to i that i be the handsome young fellow in the world
this be a matter of taste
a man of sixty may however say what he be at fourteen without much vanity and i must say i think there be some cause for my mother s opinion
the good soul s pleasure be to dress i and on sundays and holiday i turn out in a velvet coat with a silver-hilte sword by my side and a gold garter at my knee as fine as any lord in the land
my mother work i several most splendid waistcoat and i have plenty of lace for my ruffle and a fresh riband to my hair and as we walk to church on sundays even envious mrs
brady be find to allow that there be not a pretty pair in the kingdom
of course too the lady of castle brady use to sneer because on these occasion a certain tim who use to be call my valet follow i and my mother to church carry a huge prayer-book and a cane and dress in the livery of one of our own fine footman from clarges street which as tim be a bandy-shank little fellow do not exactly become he
but though poor we be gentlefolk and not to be sneer out of these become appendage to our rank and so would march up the aisle to our pew with as much state and gravity as the lord lieutenant s lady and son might do
when there my mother would give the response and amen in a loud dignified voice that be delightful to hear and besides have a fine loud voice for singing which art she have perfect in london under a fashionable teacher and she would exercise her talent in such a way that you would hardly hear any other voice of the little congregation which choose to join in the psalm
in fact my mother have great gift in every way and believe herself to be one of the most beautiful accomplished and meritorious person in the world
often and often have she talk to i and the neighbour regard her own humility and piety point they out in such a way that i would defy the most obstinate to disbelieve she
when we leave castle brady we come to occupy a house in brady s town which mamma christen barryville
i confess it be but a small place but indeed we make the most of it
i have mention the family pedigree which hang up in the drawingroom which mamma call the yellow saloon and my bedroom be call the pink bedroom and her the orange tawny apartment how well i remember they all and at dinner-time tim regularly ring a great bell and we each have a silver tankard to drink from and mother boast with justice that i have as good a bottle of claret by my side as any squire of the land
so indeed i have but i be not of course allow at my tender year to drink any of the wine which thus attain a considerable age even in the decanter
uncle brady in spite of the family quarrel find out the above fact one day by call at barryville at dinner-time and unluckily taste the liquor
you should have see how he sputter and make face
but the honest gentleman be not particular about his wine or the company in which he drink it
he would get drunk indeed with the parson or the priest indifferently with the latter much to my mother s indignation for as a true blue nassauite she heartily despise all those of the old faith and would scarcely sit down in the room with a benighted papist
but the squire have no such scruple he be indeed one of the easy idle and best- natured fellow that ever live and many an hour would he pass with the lonely widow when he be tired of madam brady at home
he like i he say as much as one of his own son and at length after the widow have hold out for a couple of year she agree to allow i to return to the castle though for herself she resolutely keep the oath which she have make with regard to her sister-in-law
the very first day i return to castle brady my trial may be say in a manner to have begin
my cousin master mick a huge monster of nineteen who hat i and i promise you i return the compliment insult i at dinner about my mother s poverty and make all the girl of the family titter
so when we go to the stable whither mick always go for his pipe of tobacco after dinner i tell he a piece of my mind and there be a fight for at least ten minute during which i stand to he like a man and black his left eye though i be myself only twelve year old at the time
of course he beat i but a beating make only a small impression on a lad of that tender age as i have prove many time in battle with the ragged brady s town boy before not one of whom at my time of life be my match
my uncle be very much pleased when he hear of my gallantry my cousin nora bring brown paper and vinegar for my nose and i go home that night with a pint of claret under my girdle not a little proud let i tell you at have hold my own against mick so long
and though he persist in his bad treatment of i and use to cane i whenever i fall in his way yet i be very happy now at castle brady with the company there and my cousin or some of they and the kindness of my uncle with whom i become a prodigious favourite
he buy a colt for i and teach i to ride
he take i out coursing and fowling and instruct i to shoot fly
and at length i be release from mick s persecution for his brother master ulick return from trinity college and hat his eld brother as be mostly the way in family of fashion take i under his protection and from that time as ulick be a deal big and strong than mick i english redmond as i be call be leave alone except when the former think fit to thrash i which he do whenever he think proper
nor be my learning neglect in the ornamental part for i have an uncommon natural genius for many thing and soon top in accomplishment most of the person around i
i have a quick ear and a fine voice which my mother cultivate to the good of her power and she teach i to step a minuet gravely and gracefully and thus lay the foundation of my future success in life
the common dance i learn as perhaps i ought not to confess in the servant hall which you may be sure be never without a piper and where i be consider unrivalled both at a hornpipe and a jig
in the matter of book-learning i have always an uncommon taste for read play and novel as the good part of a gentleman s polite education and never let a pedlar pass the village if i have a penny without have a ballad or two from he
as for your dull grammar and greek and latin and stuff i have always hat they from my youth upwards and say very unmistakably i would have none of they
this i prove pretty clearly at the age of thirteen when my aunt biddy brady s legacy of l100 come in to mamma who think to employ the sum on my education and send i to doctor tobias tickler s famous academy at ballywhacket--backwhacket as my uncle use to call it
but six week after i have be consign to his reverence i suddenly make my appearance again at castle brady have walk forty mile from the odious place and leave the doctor in a state near upon apoplexy
the fact be that at taw prison-bar or boxing i be at the head of the school but could not be bring to excel in the classic and after have be flog seven time without its do i the least good in my latin i refuse to submit altogether find it useless to an eighth application of the rod
try some other way sir say i when he be for horse i once more but he wouldnot whereon and to defend myself i fling a slate at he and knock down a scotch usher with a leaden inkstand
all the lad huzza d at this and some or the servant want to stop i but take out a large clasp-knife that my cousin nora have give i i swear i would plunge it into the waistcoat of the first man who dare to balk i and faith they let i pass on
i sleep that night twenty mile off ballywhacket at the house of a cottier who give i potato and milk and to whom i give a hundred guineas after when i come to visit ireland in my day of greatness
i wish i have the money now
but whatbe the use of regret
i have have many a hard bed than that i shall sleep on to-night and many a scantier meal than honest phil murphy give i on the evening i run away from school
so six week be all the schooling i ever get
and i say this to let parent know the value of it for though i have meet more learn book-worm in the world especially a great hulking clumsy blear-eyed old doctor whom they call johnson and who live in a court off fleet street in london yet i pretty soon silence he in an argument at button s coffeehouse and in that and in poetry and what i call natural philosophy or the science of life and in riding music leaping the small-sword the knowledge of a horse or a main of cock and the manner of an accomplish gentleman and a man of fashion i may say for myself that redmond barry have seldom find his equal
sir say i to mr
johnson on the occasion i allude to--he be accompany by a mr
buswell of scotland and i be present to the club by a mr

but i be wander from my story and must get back to home and dear old ireland again
i have make acquaintance with the good in the land since and my manner be such i have say as to make i the equal of they all and perhaps you will wonder how a country boy as i be educate amongst irish squire and their dependant of the stable and farm should arrive at possess such elegant manner as i be indisputably allow to have
i have the fact be a very valuable instructor in the person of an old gamekeeper who have serve the french king at fontenoy and who teach i the dance and custom and a smattering of the language of that country with the use of the sword both small and broad
many and many a long mile i have trudge by his side as a lad he tell i wonderful story of the french king and the irish brigade and marshal saxe and the opera- dancer he know my uncle too the chevalier borgne and indeed have a thousand accomplishment which he teach i in secret
i never know a man like he for make or throw a fly for physic a horse or breaking or choose one he teach i manly sport from birds neste upwards and i always shall consider phil purcell as the very good tutor i could have have
his fault be drink but for that i have always have a blind eye and he hat my cousin mick like poison but i could excuse he that too
with phil and at the age of fifteen i be a more accomplished man than either of my cousin and i think nature have be also more bountiful to i in the matter of person
some of the castle brady girl as you shall hear presently adore i
at fair and race many of the prettiest lass present say they would like to have i for their bachelor and yet somehow it must be confess i be not popular
in the first place every one know i be bitter poor and i think perhaps it be my good mother s fault that i be bitter proud too
i have a habit of boasting in company of my birth and the splendour of my carriage garden cellar and domestic and this before people who be perfectly aware of my real circumstance
if it be boy and they venture to sneer i would beat they or die for it and manybe the time ihave be bring home well-nigh kill by one or more of they on what when my mother ask i i would say be a family quarrel support your name with your blood reddy my boy would that saint say with the tear in her eye and so would she herself have do with her voice ay and her tooth and nail
thus at fifteen there be scarce a lad of twenty for half-a-dozen mile round that i have not beat for one cause or other
there be the vicar s two son of castle brady--in course i could not associate with such beggarly brat as they and many a battle do we have as to who should take the wall in brady s town there be pat lurgan the blacksmith s son who have the well of i four time before we come to the crowning fight when i overcome he and i could mention a score more of my deed of prowess in that way but that fisticuff fact be dull subject to talk of and to discuss before high-breed gentleman and lady
however there be another subject lady on which i must discourse and that be never out of place
day and night you like to hear of it young and old you dream and think of it
handsome and ugly and faith before fifty i never see such a thing as a plain woman itbe the subject next to the heart of all of you and i think you guess my riddle without more trouble
love
sure the word be form on purpose out of the prettiest soft vowel and consonant in the language and he or she who do not care to read about it be not worth a fig to my thinking
my uncle s family consist of ten child who as be the custom in such large family be divide into two camp or party the one side with their mamma the other take the part of my uncle in all the numerous quarrel which arise between that gentleman and his lady

patrick s dublin hence she be three- and-twenty year old at the time she and i be so much together

often and often would my dear mother make these remark concern she but i do not believe they then and somehow have get to think honoria an angelical being far above all the other angel of her sex
and as we know very well that a lady who be skilled in dancing or singing never can perfect herself without a deal of study in private and that the song or the minuet which be perform with so much graceful ease in the assembly-room have not be acquire without vast labour and perseverance in private so it be with the dear creature who be skilled in coquetting
honoria for instance be always practise and she would take poor i to rehearse her accomplishment upon or the exciseman when he come his round or the steward or the poor curate or the young apothecary s lad from brady s town whom i recollect beat once for that very reason

poor fellow

if the truth must be tell--and every word of this narrative of my life be of the most sacred veracity--my passion for nora begin in a very vulgar and unromantic way
i do not save her life on the contrary i once very nearly kill she as you shall hear

whatbe the latin for gooseberry redmond say she
she be always poke her fun as the irish phrase it

and whatbe that cry miss mysie as pert as a peacock

i be much too simple a fellow to disguise any sentiment i chance to feel in those day and not one of the eight castle brady girl but be soon aware of my passion and joke and compliment nora about her bachelor

sometimes she would treat i as a child sometimes as a man

all which vain promise of course i do not keep but i make no doubt they influence i in my very early life and cause i to do those great action for which i have be celebrate and which shall be narrate presently in order
i must tell one of they just that my dear young lady reader may know what sort of a fellow redmond barry be and what a courage and undaunted passion he have
i question whether any of the jenny- jessamine of the present day would do half as much in the face of danger
about this time it must be premise the united kingdom be in a state of great excitement from the threat generally credit of a french invasion
the pretender be say to be in high favour at versailles a descent upon ireland be especially look to and the nobleman and people of condition in that and all other part of the kingdom show their loyalty by raise regiment of horse and foot to resist the invader
brady s town send a company to join the kilwangan regiment of which master mick be the captain and we have a letter from master ulick at trinity college state that the university have also form a regiment in which he have the honour to be a corporal
how i envy they both
especially that odious mick as i see he in his laced scarlet coat with a ribbon in his hat march off at the head of his man
he the poor spiritless creature be a captain and i nothing i who feel i have as much courage as the duke of cumberland himself and feel too that a red jacket would mightily become i
my mother say i be too young to join the new regiment but the fact be that it be she herself who be too poor for the cost of a new uniform would have swallow up half her year s income and she would only have her boy appear in a way suitable to his birth rid the fine of racer dress in the good of clothe and keep the genteelest of company
well then the whole country be alive with war s alarum the three kingdom ring with military music and every man of merit pay his devoir at the court of bellona whilst poor i be oblige to stay at home in my fustian jacket and sigh for fame in secret
mr

chapter iii a false start in the genteel world i ride that night as far as carlow where i lay at the good inn and be ask what be my name by the landlord of the house give it as mr
redmond accord to my cousin s instruction and say i be of the redmonds of waterford county and be on my road to trinity college dublin to be educate there
see my handsome appearance silver-hike sword and well-fill valise my landlord make free to send up a jug of claret without my asking and charge you may be sure pretty handsomely for it in the bill
no gentleman in those good old day go to bed without a good share of liquor to set he sleep and on this my first day s entrance into the world i make a point to act the fine gentleman completely and i assure you succeed in my part to admiration
the excitement of the event of the day the quit my home the meeting with captain quin be enough to set my brain in a whirl without the claret which serve to finish i completely
i do not dream of the death of quin as some milksop perhaps would have do indeed i have never have any of that foolish remorse consequent upon any of my affair of honour always consider from the first that where a gentleman risk his own life in manly combat he be a fool to be ashamed because he win
i sleep at carlow as sound as man could sleep drink a tankard of small beer and a toast to my breakfast and exchange the first of my gold piece to settle the bill not forget to pay all the servant liberally and as a gentleman should
i begin so the first day of my life and so have continue
no man have be at great strait than i and have bear more pinch poverty and hardship but nobody can say of i that if i have a guinea i be not free-handed with it and do not spend it as well as a lord could do
i have no doubt of the future think that a man of my person part and courage could make his way anywhere
besides i have twenty gold guineas in my pocket a sum which although i be mistaken i calculate would last i for four month at least during which time something would be do towards the making of my fortune
so i ride on sing to myself or chat with the passer-by and all the girl along the road say god save i for a clever gentleman
as for nora and castle brady between to-day and yesterday there seem to be a gap as of half-a-score of year
i vow i would never re-enter the place but as a great man and i keep my vow too as you shall hear in due time
there be much more liveliness and bustle on the king s highroad in those time than in these day of stage-coach which carry you from one end of the kingdom to another in a few score hour
the gentry ride their own horse or drive in their own coach and spend three day on a journey which now occupy ten hour so that there be no lack of company for a person travel towards dublin
i make part of the journey from carlow towards naas with a well- armed gentleman from kilkenny dress in green and a gold cord with a patch on his eye and rid a powerful mare
he ask i the question of the day and whither i be bind and whether my mother be not afraid on account of the highwayman to let one so young as myself to travel
but i say pull out one of they from a holster that i have a pair of good pistol that have already do execution and be ready to do it again and here a pock-mark man come up he put spur into his bay mare and leave i
she be a much more powerful animal than mine and besides i do not wish to fatigue my horse wish to enter dublin that night and in reputable condition
as i ride towards kilcullen i see a crowd of the peasant-people assemble round a one-horse chair and my friend in green as i think make off half a mile up the hill
a footman be howl stop thief at the top of his voice but the country fellow be only laugh at his distress and make all sort of joke at the adventure which have just befall

we land at cuxhaven and before i have be a month in the electorate i be transform into a tall and proper young soldier and have a natural aptitude for military exercise be soon as accomplish at the drill as the old sergeant in the regiment
it be well however to dream of glorious war in a snug arm-chair at home ay or to make it as an officer surround by gentleman gorgeously dress and cheer by chance of promotion
but those chance do not shine on poor fellow in worsted lace the rough texture of our red coat make i ashamed when i see an officer go by my soul use to shudder when on go the round i would hear their voice as they sit jovially over the mess-table my pride revolt at be oblige to plaster my hair with flour and candle- grease instead of use the proper pomatum for a gentleman
yes my taste have always be high and fashionable and i loathe the horrid company in which i be fall
what chance have i of promotion
none of my relative have money to buy i a commission and i become soon so low-spirited that i long for a general action and a ball to finish i and vow that i would take some opportunity to desert
when i think that i the descendant of the king of ireland be threaten with a caning by a young scoundrel who have just join from eton college--when i think that he offer to make i his footman and that i do not on either occasion murder he
on the first occasion i burst into tear i do not care to own it and have serious thought of commit suicide so great be my mortification
but my kind friend fagan come to my aid in the circumstance with some very timely consolation
my poor boy say he you must not take the matter to heart so
can be only a relative disgrace
young ensign fakenham be flog himself at eton school only a month ago i would lay a wager that his scar be not yet heal
you must cheer up my boy do your duty be a gentleman and no serious harm can fall on you and i hear afterwards that my champion have take mr
fakenham very severely to task for this threat and say to he that any such proceeding for the future he should consider as an insult to himself whereon the young ensign be for the moment civil
as for the sergeant i tell one of they that if any man strike i no matter who he might be or what the penalty i would take his life
and faith
there be an air of sincerity in my speech which convince the whole bevy of they and as long as i remain in the english service no rattan be ever lay on the shoulder of redmond barry
indeed i be in that savage moody state that my mind be quite make up to the point and i look to hear my own dead march play as sure as i be alive
when i be make a corporal some of my evil be lessen i mess with the sergeant by special favour and use to treat they to drink and lose money to the rascal at play with which cash my good friend mr
fagan punctually supply i
our regiment which be quarter about stade and luneburg speedily get order to march southward towards the rhine for news come that our great general prince ferdinand of brunswick have be defeated- no not defeat but foil in his attack upon the french under the duke of broglio at bergen near frankfort-on-the-main and have be oblige to fall back
as the ally retreat the french rush forward and make a bold push for the electorate of our gracious monarch in hanover threaten that they would occupy it as they have do before when d estrees beat the hero of culloden the gallant duke of cumberland and cause he to sign the capitulation of closter zeven
an advance upon hanover always cause a great agitation in the royal bosom of the king of england more troop be send to join we convoy of treasure be pass over to our force and to our ally s the king of prussia and although in spite of all assistance the army under prince ferdinand be very much weak than that of the invade enemy yet we have the advantage of well supply one of the great general in the world and i be go to add of british valour but the less we say about that the well
my lord george sackville do not exactly cover himself with laurel at minden otherwise there might have be win there one of the great victory of modern time
throw himself between the french and the interior of the electorate prince ferdinand wisely take possession of the free town of bremen which he make his storehouse and place of arm and round which he gather all his troop make ready to fight the famous battle of minden
be these memoirs not characterise by truth and do i deign to utter a single word for which my own personal experience do not give i the full authority i might easily make myself the hero of some strange and popular adventure and after the fashion of novel-writer introduce my reader to the great character of this remarkable time
these person i mean the romance-writer if they take a drummer or a dustman for a hero somehow manage to bring he in contact with the great lord and most notorious personage of the empire and i warrant i therebe not one of they but in describe the battle of minden would manage to bring prince ferdinand and my lord george sackville and my lord granby into presence
it would have be easy for i to have say i be present when the order be bring to lord george to charge with the cavalry and finish the rout of the frenchmen and when he refuse to do so and thereby spoil the great victory
but the fact be i be two mile off from the cavalry when his lordship s fatal hesitation take place and none of we soldier of the line know of what have occur until we come to talk about the fight over our kettle in the evening and repose after the labour of a hard-fought day
i see no one of high rank that day than my colonel and a couple of orderly officer rid by in the smoke--no one on our side that be
a poor corporal as i then have the disgrace of be be not generally invite into the company of commander and the great but in revenge i see i promise you some very good company on the french part for their regiment of lorraine and royal cravate be charge we all day and in that sort of melee high and low be pretty equally receive
i hate brag but i cannot help say that i make a very close acquaintance with the colonel of the cravates for i drive my bayonet into his body and finish off a poor little ensign so young slender and small that a blow from my pigtail would have despatch he i think in place of the butt of my musket with which i club he down
i kill besides four more officer and man and in the poor ensign s pocket find a purse of fourteen louis-d or and a silver box of sugar-plum of which the former present be very agreeable to i
if people would tell their story of battle in this simple way i think the cause of truth would not suffer by it
all i know of this famous fight of minden except from book be tell here above
the ensign s silver bon-bon box and his purse of gold the livid face of the poor fellow as he fall the huzzas of the man of my company as i go out under a smart fire and rifle he their shout and curse as we come hand in hand with the frenchmen these be in truth not very dignified recollection and have well be pass over briefly
when my kind friend fagan be shoot a brother captain and his very good friend turn to lieutenant rawson and say fagan s down rawson therebe your company it be all the epitaph my brave patron get
i should have leave you a hundred guineas redmond be his last word to i but for a cursed run of ill luck last night at faro and he give i a faint squeeze of the hand then as the word be give to advance i leave he
when we come back to our old ground which we presently do he be lie there still but he be dead
some of our people have already tear off his epaulet and no doubt have rifle his purse
such knave and ruffian do man in war become
it be well for gentleman to talk of the age of chivalry but remember the starve brute whom they lead--man nurse in poverty entirely ignorant make to take a pride in deed of blood--man who can have no amusement but in drunkenness debauch and plunder
it be with these shocking instrument that your great warrior and king have be do their murderous work in the world and while for instance we be at the present moment admire the great frederick as we call he and his philosophy and his liberality and his military genius i who have serve he and be as it be behind the scene of which that great spectacle be compose can only look at it with horror
what a number of item of human crime misery slavery go to form that sum-total of glory
i can recollect a certain day about three week after the battle of minden and a farmhouse in which some of we enter and how the old woman and her daughter serve we tremble to wine and how we get drunk over the wine and the house be in a flame presently and woe betide the wretched fellow afterwards who come home to look for his house and his child
chapter v barry far from military glory after the death of my protector captain fagan i be force to confess that i fall into the very bad of course and company
be a rough soldier of fortune himself he have never be a favourite with the officer of his regiment who have a contempt for irishmen as englishmen sometimes will have and use to mock his brogue and his blunt uncouth manner
i have be insolent to one or two of they and have only be screen from punishment by his intercession especially his successor mr
rawson have no liking for i and put another man into the sergeant s place vacant in his company after the battle of minden
this act of injustice render my service very disagreeable to i and instead of seek to conquer the dislike of my superior and win their goodwill by good behaviour i only seek for mean to make my situation easy to i and grasp at all the amusement in my power
in a foreign country with the enemy before we and the people continually under contribution from one side or the other numberless irregularity be permit to the troop which would not have be allow in more peaceable time
i descend gradually to mix with the sergeant and to share their amusement drinking and gambling be i be sorry to say our principal pastime and i fall so readily into their way that though only a young lad of seventeen i be the master of they all in dare wickedness though there be some among they who i promise you be far advanced in the science of every kind of profligacy
i should have be under the provost- marshal s hand for a dead certainty have i continue much long in the army but an accident occur which take i out of the english service in rather a singular manner
the year in which george ii die our regiment have the honour to be present at the battle of warburg where the marquis of granby and his horse fully retrieve the discredit which have fall upon the cavalry since lord george sackville s defalcation at minden and where prince ferdinand once more completely defeat the frenchmen
during the action my lieutenant mr
fakenham of fakenham the gentleman who have threaten i it may be remember with the caning be strike by a musket-ball in the side
he have show no want of courage in this or any other occasion where he have be call upon to act against the french but this be his first wound and the young gentleman be exceedingly frightened by it
he offer five guineas to be carry into the town which be hard by and i and another man take he up in a cloak manage to transport he into a place of decent appearance where we put he to bed and where a young surgeon who desire nothing well than to take himself out of the fire of the musketry go presently to dress his wound
in order to get into the house we have be oblige it must be confess to fire into the lock with our piece which summon bring an inhabitant of the house to the door a very pretty and black-eyed young woman who live there with her old half-blind father a retired jagdmeister of the duke of cassel hard by
when the french be in the town meinherr s house have suffer like those of his neighbour and he be at first exceedingly unwilling to accommodate his guest
but the first knock at the door have the effect of bring a speedy answer and mr
fakenham take a couple of guineas out of a very full purse speedily convince the people that they have only to deal with a person of honour
leave the doctor who be very glad to stop with his patient who pay i the stipulate reward i be return to my regiment with my other comrade--after have pay in my german jargon some deserve compliment to the black-eyed beauty of warburg and thinking with no small envy how comfortable it would be to be billet there--when the private who be with i cut short my revery by suggest that we should divide the five guineas the lieutenant have give i
there be your share say i give the fellow one piece which be plenty as i be the leader of the expedition
but he swear a dreadful oath that he would have half and when i tell he to go to a quarter which i shall not name the fellow lift his musket hit i a blow with the butt-end of it which send i lifeless to the ground when i awake from my trance i find myself bleed with a large wound in the head and have barely time to stagger back to the house where i have leave the lieutenant when i again fall faint at the door
here i must have be discover by the surgeon on his issue out for when i awake a second time i find myself in the ground-floor of the house support by the black-eyed girl while the surgeon be copiously bleed i at the arm
there be another bed in the room where the lieutenant have be laid it be that occupy by gretel the servant while lischen as my fair one be call have till now sleep in the couch where the wound officer lay
who be you put into that bed say he languidly in german for the ball have be extract from his side with much pain and loss of blood
they tell he it be the corporal who have bring he
a corporal say he in english turn he out and you may be sure i feel highly compliment by the word
but we be both too faint to compliment or to abuse each other much and i be put to bed carefully and on be undressed have an opportunity to find that my pocket have be rifle by the english soldier after he have knock i down
however i be in good quarter the young lady who shelter i presently bring i a refreshing drink and as i take it i could not help press the kind hand that give it i nor in truth do this token of my gratitude seem unwelcome
this intimacy do not decrease with further acquaintance
i find lischen the tenderest of nurse
whenever any delicacy be to be provide for the wound lieutenant a share be always send to the bed opposite his and to the avaricious man s no small annoyance
his illness be long
on the second day the fever declare itself for some night he be delirious and i remember it be when a commanding officer be inspect our quarter with an intention very likely of billet himself on the house that the howl and mad word of the patient overhead strike he and he retire rather frightened
i have be sit up very comfortably in the low apartment for my hurt be quite subside and it be only when the officer ask i with a rough voice why i be not at my regiment that i begin to reflect how pleasant my quarter be to i and that i be much well here than crawl under an odious tent with a parcel of tipsy soldier or go the night-round or rise long before daybreak for drill
the delirium of mr
fakenham give i a hint and i determine forthwith to go mad
there be a poor fellow about brady s town call wandering billy whose insane prank i have often mimic as a lad and i again put they in practice
that night i make an attempt upon lischen salute she with a yell and a grin which frighten she almost out of her wit and when anybody come i be rave
the blow on the head have disorder my brain the doctor be ready to vouch for this fact
one night i whisper to he that i be julius caesar and consider he to be my affiance wife queen cleopatra which convince he of my insanity
indeed if her majesty have be like my aesculapius she must have have a carroty beard such as be rare in egypt
a movement on the part of the french speedily cause an advance on our part
the town be evacuate except by a few prussian troop whose surgeon be to visit the wound in the place and when we be well we be to be draft to our regiment
i determine that i never would join mine again
my intention be to make for holland almost the only neutral country of europe in those time and thence to get a passage somehow to england and home to dear old brady s town
if mr
fakenham be now alive i here tender he my apology for my conduct to he
he be very rich he use i very ill
i manage to frighten away his servant who come to attend he after the affair of warburg and from that time would sometimes condescend to wait upon the patient who always treat i with scorn but it be my object to have he alone and i bear his brutality with the utmost civility and mildness meditate in my own mind a very pretty return for all his favour to i
nor be i the only person in the house to whom the worthy gentleman be uncivil
he order the fair lischen hither and thither make impertinent love to she abuse her soup quarrel with her omelette and grudge the money which be lay out for his maintenance so that our hostess detest he as much as i think without vanity she regard i
for if the truth must be tell i have make very deep love to she during my stay under her roof as be always my way with woman of whatever age or degree of beauty
to a man who have to make his way in the world these dear girl can always be useful in one fashion or another never mind if they repel your passion at any rate they be not offend with your declaration of it and only look upon you with more favourable eye in consequence of your misfortune
as for lischen i tell she such a pathetic story of my life a tale a great deal more romantic than that here narrated for i do not restrict myself to the exact truth in that history as in these page i be bind to do that i win the poor girl s heart entirely and besides make considerable progress in the german language under her instruction
do not think i very cruel and heartless lady this heart of lischen s be like many a town in the neighbourhood in which she dwelt and have be storm and occupy several time before i come to invest it now mount french colour now green and yellow saxon now black and white prussian as the case may be
a lady who set her heart upon a lad in uniform must prepare to change lover pretty quickly or her life will be but a sad one

the reader will be able to gather from the above conversation what my design really be
i be determined to escape and to escape under the character of lieutenant fakenham take it from he to his face as it be and make use of it to meet my imperious necessity

hence it become my duty to take possession of one and the other

his paper and money however he keep under his pillow and as i have purchase a horse it become necessary to pay for it

i take at first the way towards bremen where our army be and give out that i be bring report and letter from the prussian commandant of warburg to headquarters but as soon as i get out of sight of the advanced sentinel i turn bridle and ride into the hesse-cassel territory which be luckily not very far from warburg and i promise you i be very glad to see the blue-and-red stripe on the barrier which show i that i be out of the land occupy by our countryman

he would sell to any bidder and during the five year which the war afterwards call the seven years war have now last have so exhaust the male of his principality that the field remain untilled even the child of twelve year old be drive off to the war and i see herd of these wretch march forwards attend by a few trooper now under the guidance of a red-coated hanovarian sergeant now with a prussian sub-officer accompany they with some of whom my companion exchange sign of recognition

the great and illustrious frederick have score of these white slave- dealer all round the frontier of his kingdom debauch troop or kidnap peasant and hesitate at no crime to supply those brilliant regiment of his with food for powder and i cannot help tell here with some satisfaction the fate which ultimately befell the atrocious scoundrel who violate all the right of friendship and good-fellowship have just succeed in entrap i
this individual be a person of high family and know talent and courage but who have a propensity to gambling and extravagance and find his calling as a recruit-decoy far more profitable to he than his pay of second captain in the line
the sovereign too probably find his service more useful in the former capacity
his name be monsieur de galgenstein and he be one of the most successful of the practiser of his rascally trade
he speak all language and know all country and hence have no difficulty in find out the simple braggadocio of a young lad like i
about 1765 however he come to his justly merit end
he be at this time live at kehl opposite strasburg and use to take his walk upon the bridge there and get into conversation with the french advanced sentinel to whom he be in the habit of promise mountain and marvel as the french say if they would take service in prussia
one day there be on the bridge a superb grenadier whom galgenstein accost and to whom he promise a company at least if he would enlist under frederick
ask my comrade yonder say the grenadier i can do nothing without he
we be bear and breed together we be of the same company sleep in the same room and always go in pair

but the grenadier bring his bayonet to the prussian s breast and bade he stand that he be his prisoner
the prussian however see his danger make a bind across the bridge and into the rhine whither fling aside his musket the intrepid sentry follow he
the frenchman be the well swimmer of the two seize upon the recruiter and bear he to the strasburg side of the stream where he give he up

the king prefer to reward you and the man receive money and promotion

but the king though he employ man of this stamp officer to seduce the subject of his ally could not acknowledge his own shame

turn he into the cart with the rest say he as soon as i awake from my trance
chapter vi the crimp waggon--military episodes the covered waggon to which i be order to march be stand as i have say in the courtyard of the farm with another dismal vehicle of the same kind hard by it
each be pretty well fill with a crew of man whom the atrocious crimp who have seize upon i have enlist under the banner of the glorious frederick and i could see by the lantern of the sentinel as they thrust i into the straw a dozen dark figure huddle together in the horrible move prison where i be now to be confine
a scream and a curse from my opposite neighbour show i that he be most likely wound as i myself be and during the whole of the wretched night the moans and sob of the poor fellow in similar captivity keep up a continual painful chorus which effectually prevent my get any relief from my ill in sleep
at midnight as far as i could judge the horse be put to the waggon and the creaking lumber machine be put in motion
a couple of soldier strongly arm sit on the outer bench of the cart and their grim face peer in with their lantern every now and then through the canvas curtain that they might count the number of their prisoner
the brute be half-drunk and be sing love and war song such as o gretchen mein taubchen mein herzenstrompet mein kanon mein heerpauk und meine musket prinz eugen der edle ritter and the like their wild whoop and jodel make doleful discord with the groan of we captive within the waggon
many a time afterwards have i hear these ditty sing on the march or in the barrack-room or round the fire as we lie out at night
i be not near so unhappy in spite of all as i have be on my first enlist in ireland
at least think i if i be degrade to be a private soldier there will be no one of my acquaintance who will witness my shame and that be the point which i have always care for most
there will be no one to say there be young redmond barry the descendant or the barrys the fashionable young blood of dublin pipeclaye his belt and carry his brown bess indeed but for that opinion of the world with which it be necessary that every man of spirit should keep upon equal term i for my part would have always be content with the humblest portion
now here to all intent and purpose one be as far remove from the world as in the wild of siberia or in robinson crusoe s island
and i reason with myself thus now you be catch there be no use in repining make the good of your situation and get all the pleasure you can out of it
there be a thousand opportunity of plunder c offer to the soldier in war-time out of which he can get both pleasure and profit make use of these and be happy
besides you be extraordinarily brave handsome and clever and who know but you may procure advancement in your new service in this philosophical way i look at my misfortune determine not to be cast down by they and bore woe and my broken head with perfect magnanimity
the latter be for the moment an evil against which it require no small power of endurance to contend for the jolt of the waggon be dreadful and every shake cause a throb in my brain which i think would have split my skull
as the morning dawn i see that the man next i a gaunt yellow-haired creature in black have a cushion of straw under his head
be you wound comrade say i
praise be the lord say he i be sore hurt in spirit and body and bruise in many member wounded however be i not
and you poor youth i be wound in the head say i and i want your pillow give it i--i ve a clasp-knife in my pocket and with this i give he a terrible look mean to say and mean it i do for look you a la guerre c est a la guerre and i be none of your milksop that unless he yield i the accommodation i would give he a taste of my steel
i would give it thee without any threat friend say the yellow- haired man meekly and hand i over his little sack of straw
he then lean himself back as comfortably as he could against the cart and begin repeat ein feste burg ist unser gott by which i conclude that i have get into the company of a parson
with the jolt of the waggon and accident of the journey various more exclamation and movement of the passenger show what a motley company we be
every now and then a countryman would burst into tear a french voice would be hear to say o mon dieu mon dieu a couple more of the same nation be jabber oath and chattering incessantly and a certain allusion to his own and everybody else s eye which come from a stalwart figure at the far corner tell i that there be certainly an englishman in our crew
but i be spar soon the tedium and discomfort of the journey
in spite of the clergyman s cushion my head which be throb with pain be bring abruptly in contact with the side of the waggon it begin to bleed afresh i become almost light-headed
i only recollect have a draught of water here and there once stop at a fortified town where an officer count us all the rest of the journey be pass in a drowsy stupor from which when i awake i find myself lie in a hospital bed with a nun in a white hood watch over i
they be in sad spiritual darkness say a voice from the bed next to i when the nun have finish her kind office and retire they be in the night of error and yet there be the light of faith in those poor creature it be my comrade of the crimp waggon his huge broad face loom out from under a white nightcap and ensconce in the bed beside

my honest friend s history be a curious one and it may be tell here in order to show of what motley material our levy be composed i be say he a saxon by birth my father be pastor of the village of pfannkuchen where i imbibe the first rudiment of knowledge
at sixteen i be now twenty-three have master the greek and latin tongue with the french english arabic and hebrew and have come into possession of a legacy of a hundred rixdaler a sum amply sufficient to defray my university course i go to the famous academy of gottingen where i devote four year to the exact science and theology
also i learn what worldly accomplishment i could command take a dancing-tutor at the expense of a groschen a lesson a course of fencing from a french practitioner and attend lecture on the great horse and the equestrian science at the hippodrome of a celebrated cavalry professor
my opinion be that a man should know everything as far as in his power lie that he should complete his cycle of experience and one science be as necessary as another it behove he
i be not of a save turn hence my little fortune of a hundred rixdaler which have serve to keep many a prudent man for a score of year barely suffice for five year study after which my study be interrupt my pupil fall off and i be oblige to devote much time to shoe-bind in order to save money and at a future period resume my academic course
during this period i contract an attachment here the candidate sigh a little with a person who though not beautiful and forty year of age be yet likely to sympathise with my existence and a month since my kind friend and patron university prorector doctor nasenbrumm have inform i that the pfarrer of rumpelwitz be dead ask whether i would like to have my name place upon the candidate list and if i be minded to preach a trial sermon
as the gaining of this living would further my union with my amalia i joyously consent and prepare a discourse

pardon i for put so many i be in my discourse say the candidate but when a man be talk of himself tis the brief and simple way of talk in which perhaps though i hate egotism i think my friend be right

many a gallant man of the high honour be often not proof against these and have be know to despair over a bad dinner or to be cast down at a ragged- elbow coat
my maxim be to bear all to put up with water if you cannot get burgundy and if you have no velvet to be content with frieze
but burgundy and velvet be the good bien entendu and the man be a fool who will not seize the good when the scramble be open
the head of the sermon which my friend the theologian intend to impart to i be however never tell for after our come out of the hospital he be draft into a regiment quarter as far as possible from his native country in pomerania while i be put into the bulow regiment of which the ordinary headquarters be berlin
the prussian regiment seldom change their garrison as ours do for the fear of desertion be so great that it become necessary to know the face of every individual in the service and in time of peace man live and die in the same town
this do not add as may be imagine to the amusement of the soldier s life
it be lest any young gentleman like myself should take a fancy to a military career and fancy that of a private soldier a tolerable one that i be give these i hope moral description of what we poor fellow in the rank really suffer
as soon as we recover we be dismiss from the nun and the hospital to the town prison of fulda where we be keep like slave and criminal with artilleryman with light match at the door of the courtyard and the huge black dormitory where some hundred of we lay until we be despatch to our different destination
it be soon see by the exercise which be the old soldier amongst we and which the recruit and for the former while we lay in prison there be a little more leisure though if possible a still more strict watch keep than over the break-spirited yokel who have be force or coax into the service
to describe the character here assemble would require mr
gilray s own pencil
there be man of all nation and calling
the englishmen box and bully the frenchman play card and dance and fence the heavy germans smoke their pipe and drank beer if they could manage to purchase it
those who have anything to risk gamble and at this sport i be pretty lucky for not have a penny when i enter the depot have be rob of every farthing of my property by the rascally crimp i win near a dollar in my very first game at card with one of the frenchmen who do not think of ask whether i could pay or not upon lose
such at least be the advantage of have a gentlemanlike appearance it have save i many a time since by procure i credit when my fortune be at their low ebb
among the frenchman there be a splendid man and soldier whose real name we never know but whose ultimate history create no small sensation when it come to be know in the prussian army
if beauty and courage be proof of nobility as although i have see some of the ugly dog and the great coward in the world in the noblesse i have no doubt courage and beauty be this frenchman must have be of the high family in france so grand and noble be his manner so superb his person
he be not quite so tall as myself fair while i be dark and if possible rather broad in the shoulder
he be the only man i ever meet who could master i with the small-sword with which he would pink i four time to my three
as for the sabre i could knock he to piece with it and i could leap far and carry more than he could
this however be mere egotism
this frenchman with whom i become pretty intimate-- for we be the two cock as it be of the depot and neither have any feeling of low jealousy--be call for want of a well name le blondin on account of his complexion
he be not a deserter but have come in from the lower rhine and the bishopric as i fancy fortune have prove unfavourable to he at play probably and other mean of existence be deny he
i suspect that the bastile be wait for he in his own country have he take a fancy to return thither
he be passionately fond of play and liquor and thus we have a considerable sympathy together when excite by one or the other he become frightful
i for my part can bear without wince both ill luck and wine hence my advantage over he be considerable in our bout and i win enough money from he to make my position tenable
he have a wife outside who i take it be the cause of his misfortune and separation from his family and she use to be admit to see he twice or thrice a week and never come empty- hand---a little brown bright-eyed creature whose ogle have make the great impression upon all the world
this man be draft into a regiment that be quarter at neiss in silesia which be only at a short distance from the austrian frontier he maintain always the same character for daring and skill and be in the secret republic of the regiment--which always exist as well as the regular military hierarchy--the acknowledge leader
he be an admirable soldier as i have say but haughty dissolute and a drunkard
a man of this mark unless he take care to coax and flatter his officer which i always do be sure to fall out with they
le blondin s captain be his sworn enemy and his punishment be frequent and severe
his wife and the woman of the regiment this be after the peace use to carry on a little commerce of smuggle across the austrian frontier where their dealing be wink at by both party and in obedience to the instruction of her husband this woman from every one of her excursion would bring in a little powder and ball commodity which be not to be procure by the prussian soldier and which be stow away in secret till want
they be to be want and that soon
le blondin have organise a great and extraordinary conspiracy
we donot know how far it go how many hundred or thousand it embrace but strange be the story tell about the plot amongst we private for the news be spread from garrison to garrison and talk of by the army in spite of all the government effort to hush it up--hush it up indeed
i have be of the people myself i have see the irish rebellion and i know what be the free-masonry of the poor
he make himself the head of the plot
there be no writing nor paper
no single one of the conspirator communicate with any other than the frenchman but personally he give his order to they all
he have arrange matter for a general rise of the garrison at twelve o clock on a certain day the guard-house in the town be to be seize the sentinel cut down and--who know the rest
some of our people use to say that the conspiracy be spread through all silesia and that le blondin be to be make a general in the austrian service
at twelve o clock and opposite the guard-house by the bohmer-thor of neiss some thirty man be lounge about in their undress and the frenchman stand near the sentinel of the guard-house sharpen a wood hatchet on a stone
at the stroke of twelve he get up split open the sentinel s head with a blow of his axe and the thirty man rush into the guard-house take possession of the arm there and march at once to the gate
the sentry there try to drop the bar but the frenchman rush up to he and with another blow of the axe cut off his right hand with which he hold the chain
see the man rush out armed the guard without the gate draw up across the road to prevent their passage but the frenchman s thirty give they a volley charge they with the bayonet and bring down several and the rest fly the thirty rush on
the frontier be only a league from neiss and they make rapidly towards it
but the alarm be give in the town and what save it be that the clock by which the frenchman go be a quarter of an hour fast than any of the clock in the town
the generale be beat the troop call to arm and thus the man who be to have attack the other guard-house be oblige to fall into the rank and their project be defeat
this however likewise render the discovery of the conspirator impossible for no man could betray his comrade nor of course would he criminate himself
cavalry be send in pursuit of the frenchman and his thirty fugitive who be by this time far on their way to the bohemian frontier
when the horse come up with they they turn receive they with a volley and the bayonet and drive they back
the austrians be out at the barrier look eagerly on at the conflict
the woman who be on the look-out too bring more ammunition to these intrepid deserter and they engage and drive back the dragoon several time
but in these gallant and fruitless combat much time be lose and a battalion presently come up and surround the brave thirty when the fate of the poor fellow be decide
they fight with the fury of despair not one of they ask for quarter
when their ammunition fail they fight with the steel and be shoot down or bayonet where they stand
the frenchman be the very last man who be hit
he receive a bullet in the thigh and fall and in this state be overpower kill the officer who first advance to seize he
he and the very few of his comrade who survive be carry back to neiss and immediately as the ringleader he be bring before a council of war
he refuse all interrogation which be make as to his real name and family
what matter who i be say he you have i and will shoot i
my name would not save i be it ever so famous in the same way he decline to make a single discovery regard the plot
it be all my doing he say each man engage in it only know i and be ignorant of every one of his comrade
the secret be mine alone and the secret shall die with i when the officer ask he what be the reason which induce he to meditate a crime so horrible it be your infernal brutality and tyranny he say
you be all butcher ruffian tiger and you owe it to the cowardice of your man that you be not murder long ago at this his captain burst into the most furious exclamation against the wounded man and rush up to he strike he a blow with his fist
but le blondin wound as he be as quick as thought seize the bayonet of one of the soldier who support he and plunge it into the officer s breast
scoundrel and monster say he i shall have the consolation of send you out of the world before i die he be shoot that day
he offer to write to the king if the officer would agree to let his letter go seal into the hand of the postmaster but they fear no doubt that something might be say to inculpate themselves and refuse he the permission

however it be the king s interest to conceal the matter and so it be as i have say before hush up--so well hush up that a hundred thousand soldier in the army know it and manybe the one of we that have drink to the frenchman s memory over our wine as a martyr for the cause of the soldier
i shall have doubtless some reader who will cry out at this that i be encouraging insubordination and advocate murder
if these man have serve as private in the prussian army from 1760 to 1765 they would not be so apt to take objection
this man destroy two sentinel to get his liberty how many hundred of thousand of his own and the austrian people do king frederick kill because he take a fancy to silesia
it be the accursed tyranny of the system that sharpen the axe which brain the two sentinel of neiss and so let officer take warning and think twice ere they visit poor fellow with the cane
i could tell many more story about the army but as from have be a soldier myself all my sympathy be in the rank no doubt my tale would be pronounce to be of an immoral tendency and i have well therefore be brief

go to the inspect officer say i if they once get you into prussia it be all over with you and they will never give you up
