fakenham find mean to be allow to go into hospital and while in hospital the matter be arrange as i have recommend
he have nearly however miss his freedom by his own stinginess in bargaining for it and never show the least gratitude towards i his benefactor
i be not go to give any romantic narrative of the seven years war
at the close of it the prussian army so renowned for its discipline valour be officer and under-officer by native prussians it be true but be compose for the most part of man hire or steal like myself from almost every nation in europe
the deserting to and fro be prodigious
in my regiment bulow s alone before the war there have be no less than 600 frenchmen and as they march out of berlin for the campaign one of the fellow have an old fiddle on which he be flay a french tune and his comrade dance almost rather than walk after he singing nous allon en france two year after when they return to berlin there be only six of these man leave the rest have flee or be kill in action
the life the private soldier lead be a frightful one to any but man of iron courage and endurance
there be a corporal to every three man march behind they and pitilessly use the cane so much so that it use to be say that in action there be a front rank of private and a second rank of sergeant and corporal to drive they on
many man would give way to the most frightful act of despair under these incessant persecution and torture and amongst several regiment of the army a horrible practice have spring up which for some time cause the great alarm to the government
this be a strange frightful custom of child-murder
the man use to say that life be unbearable that suicide be a crime in order to avert which and to finish with the intolerable misery of their position the good plan be to kill a young child which be innocent and therefore secure of heaven and then to deliver themselves up as guilty of the murder
the king himself--the hero sage and philosopher the prince who have always liberality on his lip and who affect a horror of capital punishment--be frighten at this dreadful protest on the part of the wretch whom he have kidnap against his monstrous tyranny but his only mean of remedy the evil be strictly to forbid that such criminal should be attend by any ecclesiastic whatever and deny all religious consolation
the punishment be incessant
every officer have the liberty to inflict it and in peace it be more cruel than in war
for when peace come the king turn adrift such of his officer as be not noble whatever their service might have be
he would call a captain to the front of his company and say he be not noble let he go we be afraid of he somehow and be cow before he like wild beast before their keeper
i have see the brave man of the army cry like child at a cut of the cane i have see a little ensign of fifteen call out a man of fifty from the rank a man who have be in a hundred battle and he have stand present arm and sob and howl like a baby while the young wretch lash he over the arm and thigh with the stick
in a day of action this man would dare anything
a button might be awry then and nobody touch he but when they have make the brute fight then they lash he again into subordination
almost all of we yield to the spell--scarce one could break it
the french officer i have speak of as take along with i be in my company and can like a dog
i meet he at versailles twenty year afterwards and he turn quite pale and sick when i speak to he of old day
for god s sake say he donot talk of that time i wake up from my sleep tremble and cry even now as for i after a very brief time in which it must be confess i taste like my comrade of the cane and after i have find opportunity to show myself to be a brave and dexterous soldier i take the mean i have adopt in the english army to prevent any further personal degradation
i wear a bullet around my neck which i do not take the pain to conceal and i give out that it should be for the man or officer who cause i to be chastise
and there be something in my character which make my superior believe i for that bullet have already serve i to kill an austrian colonel and i would have give it to a prussian with as little remorse
for what care i for their quarrel or whether the eagle under which i march have one head or two
all i say be no man shall find i trip in my duty but no man shall ever lay a hand upon i and by this maxim i abide as long as i remain in the service
i do not intend to make a history of battle in the prussian any more than in the english service
i do my duty in they as well as another and by the time that my moustache have grow to a decent length which it do when i be twenty year of age there be not a braver cleverer handsomer and i must own wickeder soldier in the prussian army
i have form myself to the condition of the proper fighting beast on a day of action i be savage and happy out of the field i take all the pleasure i could get and be by no mean delicate as to its quality or the manner of procure it
the truth be however that there be among our man a much high tone of society than among the clumsy lout in the english army and our service be generally so strict that we have little time for do mischief
i be very dark and swarthy in complexion and be call by our fellow the black englander the schwartzer englander or the english devil
if any service be to be do i be sure to be put upon it
i get frequent gratification of money but no promotion and it be on the day after i have kill the austrian colonel a great officer of uhlans whom i engage--singly and on foot that general bulow my colonel give i two frederics-d or in front of the regiment and say i reward thee now but i fear i shall have to hang thee one day or other i spend the money and that i have take from the colonel s body every groschen that night with some jovial companion but as long as war last be never without a dollar in my purse
chapter vii barry leads a garrison life and find many friend there after the war our regiment be garrison in the capital the least dull perhaps of all the town of prussia but that do not say much for its gaiety
our service which be always severe still leave many hour of the day disengage in which we might take our pleasure have we the mean of pay for the same
many of our mess get leave to work in trade but i have be bring up to none and besides my honour forbid i for as a gentleman i could not soil my finger by a manual occupation
but our pay be barely enough to keep we from starve and as i have always be fond of pleasure and as the position in which we now be in the midst of the capital prevent we from resort to those mean of levy contribution which be always pretty feasible in wartime i be oblige to adopt the only mean leave i of provide for my expense and in a word become the ordonnanz or confidential military gentleman of my captain
i spurn the office four year previously when it be make to i in the english service but the position be very different in a foreign country besides to tell the truth after five year in the rank a man s pride will submit to many rebuff which would be intolerable to he in an independent condition
the captain be a young man and have distinguish himself during the war or he would never have be advance to rank so early
he be moreover the nephew and heir of the minister of police monsieur de potzdorff a relationship which no doubt aid in the young gentleman s promotion
captain de potzdorff be a severe officer enough on parade or in barrack but he be a person easily lead by flattery
i win his heart in the first place by my manner of tie my hair in queue indeed it be more neatly dress than that of any man in the regiment and subsequently gain his confidence by a thousand little art and compliment which as a gentleman myself i know how to employ
he be a man of pleasure which he pursue more openly than most man in the stern court of the king he be generous and careless with his purse and he have a great affection for rhine wine in all which quality i sincerely sympathise with he and from which i of course have my profit
he be dislike in the regiment because he be suppose to have too intimate relation with his uncle the police minister to whom it be hint he carry the news of the corps
before long i have ingratiate myself considerably with my officer and know most of his affair
thus i be relieve from many drill and parade which would otherwise have fall to my lot and come in for a number of perquisite which enable i to support a genteel figure and to appear with some eclat in a certain though it must be confess very humble society in berlin
among the lady i be always an especial favourite and so polished be my behaviour amongst they that they could not understand how i should have obtain my frightful nickname of the black devil in the regiment
he be not so black as he be paint i laughingly would say and most of the lady agree that the private be quite as well-breed as the captain as indeed how should it be otherwise consider my education and birth
when i be sufficiently ingratiate with he i ask leave to address a letter to my poor mother in ireland to whom i have not give any news of myself for many many year for the letter of the foreign soldier be never admit to the post for fear of appeal or disturbance on the part of their parent abroad
my captain agree to find mean to forward the letter and as i know that he would open it i take care to give it he unseal thus show my confidence in he
but the letter be as you may imagine write so that the writer should come to no harm be it intercept
i beg my honoured mother s forgiveness for have flee from she i say that my extravagance and folly in my own country i know render my return thither impossible but that she would at least be glad to know that i be well and happy in the service of the great monarch in the world and that the soldier s life be most agreeable to i and i add that i have find a kind protector and patron who i hop would some day provide for i as i know it be out of her power to do
i offer remembrance to all the girl at castle brady name they from biddy to becky downward and sign myself as in truth i be her affectionate son redmond barry in captain potzdorffs company of the bulowisch regiment of foot in garrison at berlin
also i tell she a pleasant story about the king kick the chancellor and three judge downstair as he have do one day when i be on guard at potsdam and say i hop for another war soon when i might rise to be an officer
in fact you might have imagine my letter to be that of the happy fellow in the world and i be not on this head at all sorry to mislead my kind parent
i be sure my letter be read for captain potzdorff begin ask i some day afterwards about my family and i tell he the circumstance pretty truly all thing consider
i be a cadet of a good family but my mother be almost ruin and have barely enough to support her eight daughter whom i name
i have be to study for the law at dublin where i have get into debt and bad company have kill a man in a duel and would be hang or imprison by his powerful friend if i return
i have enlist in the english service where an opportunity for escape present itself to i such as i could not resist and hereupon i tell the story of mr
fakenham of fakenham in such a way as make my patron to be convulse with laughter and he tell i afterwards that he have repeat the story at madame de kamake s evening assembly where all the world be anxious to have a sight of the young englander
be the british ambassador there i ask in a tone of the great alarm and add for heaven s sake sir do not tell my name to he or he might ask to have i deliver up and i have no fancy to go to be hang in my dear native country potzdorff laughing say he would take care that i should remain where i be on which i swear eternal gratitude to he
some day afterwards and with rather a grave face he say to i redmond i have be talk to our colonel about you and as i wonder that a fellow of your courage and talent have not be advanced during the war the general say they have have their eye upon you that you be a gallant soldier and have evidently come of a good stock that no man in the regiment have have less fault find with he but that no man merit promotion less
you be idle dissolute and unprincipled you have do a deal of harm to the man and for all your talent and bravery he be sure would come to no good sir say i quite astonish that any mortal man should have form such an opinion of i i hope general bulow be mistake regard my character
i have fall into bad company it be true but i have only do as other soldier have do and above all i have never have a kind friend and protector before to whom i might show that i be worthy of well thing
the general may say i be a ruin lad and send i to the d---l but be sure of this i would go to the d---l to serve you this speech i see please my patron very much and as i be very discreet and useful in a thousand delicate way to he he soon come to have a sincere attachment for i
one day or rather night when he be tete-a-tete with the lady of the tabaks rath von dose for instance i--but there be no use in tell affair which concern nobody now
four month after my letter to my mother i get under cover to the captain a reply which create in my mind a yearning after home and a melancholy which i cannot describe
i have not see the dear soul s writing for five year
all the old day and the fresh happy sunshine of the old green field in ireland and her love and my uncle and phil purcell and everything that i have do and think come back to i as i read the letter and when i be alone i cry over it as i havenot do since the day when nora jilt i
i take care not to show my feeling to the regiment or my captain but that night when i be to have take tea at the garden-house outside brandenburg gate with fraulein lottchen the tabaks rathinn s gentlewoman of company i somehow have not the courage to go but beg to be excuse and go early to bed in barrack out of which i go and come now almost as i will and pass a long night weep and think about dear ireland
next day my spirit rise again and i get a ten-guinea bill cash which my mother send in the letter and give a handsome treat to some of my acquaintance
the poor soul s letter be blot all over with tear full of text and write in the wildest incoherent way
she say she be delighted to think i be under a protestant prince though she fear he be not in the right way that right way she say she have the blessing to find under the guidance of the reverend joshua jowls whom she sit under
she say he be a precious choose vessel a sweet ointment and precious box of spikenard and make use of a great number more phrase that i could not understand but one thing be clear in the midst of all this jargon that the good soul love her son still and thought and pray day and night for her wild redmond
have it not come across many a poor fellow in a solitary night s watch or in sorrow sickness or captivity that at that very minute most likely his mother be pray for he
i often have have these thought but they be none of the gayest and itbe quite as well that they donot come to you in company for where would be a set of jolly fellow then as mute as undertaker at a funeral i promise you

jowls be very wroth with she

when the captain and the lady fall out and he begin to pay his address to the rich daughter of the dutch minister i donot know how many more letter and guinea the unfortunate tabaks rathinn hand over to i that i might get her lover back again

as for the sergeant they be as civil to i as to an officer it be as much as their stripe be worth to offend a person who have the ear of the minister s nephew
there be in my company a young fellow by the name of kurz who be six foot high in spite of his name and whose life i have save in some affair of the war
what do this lad do after i have recount to he one of my adventure but call i a spy and informer and beg i not to call he du any more as be the fashion with young man when they be very intimate
i have nothing for it but to call he out but i owe he no grudge
i disarm he in a twinkling and as i send his sword fly over his head say to he kurz do ever you know a man guilty of a mean action who can do as i do now this silence the rest of the grumbler and no man ever sneer at i after that
no man can suppose that to a person of my fashion the wait in antechamber the conversation of footman and hanger-on be pleasant
but it be not more degrading than the barrack-room of which i need not say i be heartily sick
my protestation of like for the army be all intend to throw dust into the eye of my employer

i know i be bear to make a figure in the world

my plan be this i may make myself so necessary to m
de potzdorff that he will obtain my freedom

and i be grateful to my employer more grateful than they to i

some prudish person may affect indignation at the frankness of these confession but heaven pity they
do you suppose that any man who have lose or win a hundred thousand pound at play will not take the advantage which his neighbour enjoy
they be all the same
but it be only the clumsy fool who cheats who resort to the vulgar expedient of cogged dice and cut card
such a man be sure to go wrong some time or other and be not fit to play in the society of gallant gentleman and my advice to people who see such a vulgar person at his prank be of course to back he while he play but never--never to have anything to do with he
play grandly honourably
be not of course cast down at lose but above all be not eager at win as mean soul be

i warrant the legend of the heralds college be not more authentic than mine be

we be in partnership with a florentine well know in all the courts of europe the count alessandro pippi as skilful a player as ever be see but he turn out a sad knave latterly and i have discover that his countship be a mere imposture

we always play on parole with anybody any person that be of honour and noble lineage
we never press for our winning or decline to receive promissory note in lieu of gold
but woe to the man who do not pay when the note become due
redmond de balibari be sure to wait upon he with his bill and i promise you there be very few bad debt on the contrary gentleman be grateful to we for our forbearance and our character for honour stand unimpeached
in later time a vulgar national prejudice have choose to cast a slur upon the character of man of honour engage in the profession of play but i speak of the good old day in europe before the cowardice of the french aristocracy in the shameful revolution which serve they right bring discredit and ruin upon our order
they cry fie now upon man engage in play but i should like to know how much more honourable their mode of livelihood be than our

the merchant who deal in tea and tallow be he any well
his bale of dirty indigo be his dice his card come up every year instead of every ten minute and the sea be his green table

the princess frederica amelia who be affect by my success and shall always think with gratitude of the protection with which that exalt lady honour i

the point of honour be not understand by the charming sex and it be with the great difficulty in our peregrination to the various courts of northern europe that we could keep they from the table could get their money if they lose or if they pay prevent they from use the most furious and extraordinary mean of revenge
in those great day of our fortune i calculate that we lose no less than fourteen thousand louis by such failure of payment
a princess of a ducal house give we paste instead of diamond which she have solemnly pledge to we another organise a robbery of the crown jewel and would have charge the theft upon we but for pippi s caution who have keep back a note of hand her high transparency give we and send it to his ambassador by which precaution i do believe our neck be save
a third lady of high but not princely rank after i have win a considerable sum in diamond and pearl from she send her lover with a band of cut-throat to waylay i and it be only by extraordinary courage skill and good luck that i escape from these villain wound myself but leave the chief aggressor dead on the ground my sword enter his eye and break there and the villain who be with he flee see their chief fall
they might have finish i else for i have no weapon of defence
thus it will be see that our life for all its splendour be one of extreme danger and difficulty require high talent and courage for success and often when we be in a full vein of success we be suddenly drive from our ground on account of some freak of a reign prince some intrigue of a disappointed mistress or some quarrel with the police minister
if the latter personage be not bribe or win over nothing be more common than for we to receive a sudden order of departure and so perforce we live a wandering and desultory life
though the gain of such a life be as i have say very great yet the expense be enormous
our appearance and retinue be too splendid for the narrow mind of pippi who be always cry out at my extravagance though oblige to own that his own meanness and parsimony would never have achieve the great victory which my generosity have win
with all our success our capital be not very great
that speech to the duke of courland for instance be a mere boast as far as the two hundred thousand florin at three month be concern
we have no credit and no money beyond that on our table and should have be force to fly if his highness have win and accept our bill
sometimes too we be hit very hard
a bank be a certainty almost but now and then a bad day will come and man who have the courage of good fortune at least ought to meet bad luck well the former believe i be the hard task of the two
one of these evil chance befell we in the duke of baden s territory at mannheim
pippi who be always on the look-out for business offer to make a bank at the inn where we put up and where the officer of the duke s cuirassier sup and some small play accordingly take place and some wretched crown and louis change hand i trust rather to the advantage of these poor gentleman of the army who be surely the poor of all devil under the sun
but as ill luck would have it a couple of young student from the neighbouring university of heidelberg who have come to mannheim for their quarter s revenue and so have some hundred of dollar between they be introduce to the table and have never play before begin to win as be always the case
as ill luck would have it too they be tipsy and against tipsiness i have often find the good calculation of play fail entirely
they play in the most perfectly insane way and yet win always
every card they back turn up in their favour
they have win a hundred louis from we in ten minute and see that pippi be grow angry and the luck against we i be for shut up the bank for the night say the play be only mean for a joke and that now we have have enough
but pippi who have quarrel with i that day be determined to proceed and the upshot be that the student play and win more then they lend money to the officer who begin to win too and in this ignoble way in a tavern room thick with tobacco-smoke across a deal table besmear with beer and liquor and to a parcel of hungry subaltern and a pair of beardless student three of the most skilful and renowned player in europe lose seventeen hundred louis
i blush now when i think of it

johnson write and be in fact a most shameful defeat

thus after eighteen month we have to begin the world again

ah
i suffer from none of these ill in the year 66 when there be no man in europe more gay in spirit more splendid in personal accomplishment than young redmond barry

among the ecclesiastical principality of the rhine we be particularly well receive
i never know finer or gayer courts than those of the electors of treves and cologne where there be more splendour and gaiety than at vienna far more than in the wretched barrack-court of berlin
the court of the archduchess-governess of the netherlands be likewise a royal place for us knight of the dice-box and gallant votary of fortune whereas in the stingy dutch or the beggarly swiss republic it be impossible for a gentleman to gain a livelihood unmoleste
after our mishap at mannheim my uncle and i make for the duchy of x---
the reader may find out the place easily enough but i do not choose to print at full the name of some illustrious person in whose society i then fall and among whom i be make the sharer in a very strange and tragical adventure
there be no court in europe at which stranger be more welcome than at that of the noble duke of x--- none where pleasure be more eagerly seek after and more splendidly enjoy
the prince do not inhabit his capital of s--- but imitate in every respect the ceremonial of the court of versailles build himself a magnificent palace at a few league from his chief city and round about his palace a superb aristocratic town inhabit entirely by his noble and the officer of his sumptuous court
the people be rather hardly press to be sure in order to keep up this splendour for his highness s dominion be small and so he wisely live in a sort of awful retirement from they seldom show his face in his capital or see any countenance but those of his faithful domestic and officer
his palace and garden of ludwigslust be exactly on the french model
twice a week there be court reception and grand court gala twice a month
there be the fine opera out of france and a ballet unrivalle in splendour on which his highness a great lover of music and dancing expend prodigious sum
it may be because i be then young but i think i never see such an assemblage of brilliant beauty as use to figure there on the stage of the court theatre in the grand mythological ballet which be then the mode and in which you see mars in red- heel pump and a periwig and venus in patch and a hoop
they say the costume be incorrect and have change it since but for my part i have never see a venus more lovely than the coralie who be the chief dancer and find no fault with the attendant nymph in their train and lappet and powder
these opera use to take place twice a week after which some great officer of the court would have his evening and his brilliant supper and the dice-box rattle everywhere and all the world play
i have see seventy play-table set out in the grand gallery of ludwigslust besides the faro-bank where the duke himself would graciously come and play and win or lose with a truly royal splendour

in that noble court everybody be a gambler

chapter xiii i continue my career as a man of fashion i find i have already fill up many score of page and yet a vast deal of the most interesting portion of my history remain to be tell viz
that which describe my sojourn in the kingdom of england and ireland and the great part i play there move among the most illustrious of the land myself not the least distinguish of the brilliant circle
in order to give due justice to this portion of my memoirs then which be more important than my foreign adventure can be though i could fill volume with interesting description of the latter i shall cut short the account of my travel in europe and of my success at the continental courts in order to speak of what befell i at home
suffice it to say that there be not a capital in europe except the beggarly one of berlin where the young chevalier de balibari be not know and admire and where he have not make the brave the high-bear and the beautiful talk of he
i win 80 000 rouble from potemkin at the winter palace at petersburg which the scoundrelly favourite never pay i i have have the honour of see his royal highness the chevalier charles edward as drunk as any porter at rome my uncle play several match at billiard against the celebrate lord c----at spa and i promise you do not come off a loser

adventurer

my profession be play in which i be then unrivalle
no man could play with i through europe on the square and my income be just as certain during health and the exercise of my profession as that of a man who draw on his three- per-cent or any fat squire whose acre bring he revenue
harvest be not more certain than the effect of skill be a crop be a chance as much as a game of card greatly play by a fine player there may be a drought or a frost or a hail-storm and your stake be lose but one man be just as much an adventurer as another

it be always the good way

i have play at the bullet game too much in earnest to make use of it heedlessly and i may say proudly for myself that i never engage in a duel unless i have a real available and prudent reason for it
there be a simplicity about this irish gentry which amuse and make i wonder

but i must tell you what she do which be so clever
why it be the simple thing in the world as all master-stroke be

i will do everything i say which you ask of i except when you bid i not to love you
that be beyond my power and while my heart have a pulse i must follow you
it be my fate your fate
cease to battle against it and be mine
loveliest of your sex

a year from that day on the 15 th of may in the year 1773 i have the honour and happiness to lead to the altar honoria countess of lyndon widow of the late right honourable sir charles lyndon k b

after we have receive the congratulation of our friend in london-- for in those day people be not ashamed of be marry as they seem to be now--i and honoria who be all complacency and a most handsome sprightly and agreeable companion set off to visit our estate in the west of england where i have never as yet set foot
we leave london in three chariot each with four horse and my uncle would have be pleased could he have see paint on their panel the irish crown and the ancient coat of the barrys beside the countess s coronet and the noble cognisance of the noble family of lyndon
before quit london i procure his majesty s gracious permission to add the name of my lovely lady to my own and henceforward assume the style and title of barry lyndon as i have write it in this autobiography
chapter xvii i appear as an ornament of english society all the journey down to hackton castle the large and most ancient of our ancestral seat in devonshire be perform with the slow and sober state become people of the first quality in the realm
an outrider in my livery go on before we and bespoke our lodging from town to town and thus we lay in state at andover ilminster and exeter and the fourth evening arrive in time for supper before the antique baronial mansion of which the gate be in an odious gothic taste that would have set mr
walpole wild with pleasure
the first day of a marriage be commonly very try and i have know couple who live together like turtle-dove for the rest of their life peck each other s eye out almost during the honeymoon
i do not escape the common lot in our journey westward my lady lyndon choose to quarrel with i because i pull out a pipe of tobacco the habit of smoking which i have acquire in germany when a soldier in billow s and could never give it over and smoke it in the carriage and also her ladyship choose to take umbrage both at ilminster and andover because in the evening when we lay there i choose to invite the landlord of the bell and the lion to crack a bottle with i
lady lyndon be a haughty woman and i hate pride and i promise you that in both instance i overcome this vice in she
on the third day of our journey i have she to light my pipematch with her own hand and make she deliver it to i with tear in her eye and at the swan inn at exeter i have so completely subdue she that she ask i humbly whether i would not wish the landlady as well as the host to step up to dinner with we

this conduct at the very outset of my career in the county make i not a little popular and the residentiary canon who do i the favour to sup with i at the inn go away after the sixth bottle hiccup the most solemn vow for the welfare of such a p-p-pious gentleman
before we reach hackton castle we have to drive through ten mile of the lyndon estate where the people be out to visit we the church bell set a-ringing the parson and the farmer assemble in their good by the roadside and the school child and the labour people be loud in their hurrah for her ladyship
i fling money among these worthy character stop to bow and chat with his reverence and the farmer and if i find that the devonshire girl be among the handsome in the kingdom be it my fault

i wish they have be oak when i cut the tree down in 79 for they would have fetch three time the money i know nothing more culpable than the carelessness of ancestor in plant their ground with timber of small value when they might just as easily raise oak

for the first few day after our arrival my time be agreeably spend in receive the visit of the nobility and gentry who come to pay their respect to the noble new-married couple and like bluebeard s wife in the fairy tale in inspect the treasure the furniture and the numerous chamber of the castle
it be a huge old place build as far back as henry v s time besiege and batter by the cromwellians in the revolution and alter and patch up in an odious old-fashioned taste by the roundhead lyndon who succeed to the property at the death of a brother whose principle be excellent and of the true cavalier sort but who ruin himself chiefly by drinking dice and a dissolute life and a little by support the king
the castle stand in a fine chase which be prettily speckle over with deer and i cannot but own that my pleasure be considerable at first as i sit in the oak parlour of summer evening with the window open the gold and silver plate shin in a hundred dazzle colour on the side-board a dozen jolly companion round the table and could look out over the wide green park and the wave wood and see the sun set on the lake and hear the deer call to one another
the exterior be when i first arrive a quaint composition of all sort of architecture of feudal tower and gable-end in queen bess s style and rough-patch wall build up to repair the ravage of the roundhead cannon but i need not speak of this at large have have the place new-faced at a vast expense under a fashionable architect and the facade lay out in the late french- greek and most classical style
there have be moat and drawbridge and outer wall these i have shave away into elegant terrace and handsomely lay out in parterre accord to the plan of monsieur cornichon the great parisian architect who visit england for the purpose
after ascend the outer step you enter an antique hall of vast dimension wainscote with black carve oak and ornament with portrait of our ancestor from the square beard of brook lyndon the great lawyer in queen bess s time to the loose stomacher and ringlet of lady saccharissa lyndon whom vandyck paint when she be a maid of honour to queen henrietta maria and down to sir charles lyndon with his riband as a knight of the bath and my lady paint by hudson in a white satin sack and the family diamond as she be present to the old king george ii

bow and pike huge stag-head and hunting implement and rusty old suit of armour that may have be wear in the day of gog and magog for what i know form the other old ornament of this huge apartment and be range round a fireplace where you might have turn a coach-and-six
this i keep pretty much in its antique condition but have the old armour eventually turn out and consign to the lumber-room upstairs replace it with china monster gild settee from france and elegant marble of which the broken nose and limb and ugliness undeniably prove their antiquity and which an agent purchase for i at rome
but such be the taste of the time and perhaps the rascality of my agent that thirty thousand pound worth of these gem of art only go for three hundred guineas at a subsequent period when i find it necessary to raise money on my collection
from this main hall branch off on either side the long series of state-room poorly furnish with high-back chair and long queer venice glass when first i come to the property but afterwards render so splendid by i with the gold damask of lyons and the magnificent gobelin tapestry i win from richelieu at play

these change and this train de vivre require as may be suppose no small outlay and i confess that i have little of that base spirit of economy in my composition which some people practise and admire

to say the truth lady lyndon s figure and appearance be not at this time such as to make for their owner any very brilliant appearance in the fashionable world

my company from this of course fancy i be a tyrant over she whereas i be only a severe and careful guardian over a silly bad-temper and weak-minded lady
