here i have meet very queer character who come to queer end too poor hackman that afterwards be hang for kill miss reay and on the sly his reverence doctor simony whom my friend sam foote of the little theatre bade to live even after forgery and the rope cut short the unlucky parson s career
it be a merry place london in those day and thatbe the truth
ibe write now in my gouty old age and people have grow vastly more moral and matter-of-fact than they be at the close of the last century when the world be young with i
there be a difference between a gentleman and a common fellow in those time
we wear silk and embroidery then
now every man have the same coachmanlike look in his belcher and caped coat and there be no outward difference between my lord and his groom
then it take a man of fashion a couple of hour to make his toilette and he could show some taste and genius in the select it
what a blaze of splendour be a drawing-room or an opera of a gala night
what sum of money be lose and win at the delicious faro-table
my gilt curricle and out-rider blaze in green and gold be very different object from the equipage you see nowadays in the ring with the stunt groom behind they
a man could drink four time as much as the milksop nowadays can swallow but tis useless expatiating on this theme
gentleman be dead and go
the fashion have now turn upon your soldier and sailor and i grow quite moody and sad when i think of thirty year ago
this be a chapter devote to reminiscence of what be a very happy and splendid time with i but present little of mark in the way of adventure as be generally the case when time be happy and easy
it would seem idle to fill page with account of the every- day occupation of a man of fashion the fair lady who smile upon he the dress he wear the match he play and win or lose
at this period of time when youngster be employ cut the frenchmen s throat in spain and france lie out in bivouac and feed off commissariat beef and biscuit they would not understand what a life their ancestor lead and so i shall leave far discourse upon the pleasure of the time when even the prince be a lad in leading-string when charles fox have not subside into a mere statesman and buonaparte be a beggarly brat in his native island
whilst these improvement be go on in my estates my house from an antique norman castle be change to an elegant greek temple or palace--my garden and wood lose their rustic appearance to be adapt to the most genteel french style--my child grow up at his mother s knee and my influence in the country increasing it must not be imagine that i stay in devonshire all this while and that i neglect to make visit to london and my various estate in england and ireland
i go to reside at the trecothick estate and the polwellan wheal where i find instead of profit every kind of pettifog chicanery i pass over in state to our territory in ireland where i entertain the gentry in a style the lord lieutenant himself could not equal give the fashion to dublin to be sure it be a beggarly savage city in those day and since the time there have be a pother about the union and the misfortune attend it i have be at a loss to account for the mad praise of the old order of thing which the fond irish patriot have invent i say i set the fashion to dublin and small praise to i for a poor place it be in those time whatever the irish party may say
in a former chapter i have give you a description of it
it be the warsaw of our part of the world there be a splendid ruin half- civilised nobility rule over a half-savage population
i say half-savage advisedly
the commonalty in the street be wild unshorn and in rag
the most public place be not safe after nightfall
the college the public building and the great gentry s house be splendid the latter unfinished for the most part but the people be in a state more wretched than any vulgar i have ever know the exercise of their religion be only half allow to they their clergy be force to be educate out of the country their aristocracy be quite distinct from they there be a protestant nobility and in the town poor insolent protestant corporation with a bankrupt retinue of mayor aldermen and municipal officer all of whom figure in address and have the public voice in the country but there be no sympathy and connection between the upper and the low people of the irish
to one who have be breed so much abroad as myself this difference between catholic and protestant be doubly striking and though as firm as a rock in my own faith yet i could not help remember my grandfather hold a different one and wonder that there should be such a political difference between the two
i pass among my neighbour for a dangerous leveller for entertain and express such opinion and especially for ask the priest of the parish to my table at castle lyndon
he be a gentleman educate at salamanca and to my mind a far well bred and more agreeable companion than his comrade the rector who have but a dozen protestants for his congregation who be a lord s son to be sure but he could hardly spell and the great field of his labour be in the kennel and cockpit
i do not extend and beautify the house of castle lyndon as i have do our other estate but content myself with pay an occasional visit there exercise an almost royal hospitality and keep open house during my stay
when absent i give to my aunt the widow brady and her six unmarried daughter although they always detest i permission to inhabit the place my mother prefer my new mansion of barryogue
and as my lord bullingdon be by this time grow excessively tall and troublesome i determine to leave he under the care of a proper governor in ireland with mrs
brady and her six daughter to take care of he and he be welcome to fall in love with all the old lady if he be so minded and thereby imitate his stepfather s example
when tired of castle lyndon his lordship be at liberty to go and reside at my house with my mamma but there be no love lose between he and she and on account of my son bryan i think she hat he as cordially as ever i myself could possibly do
the county of devon be not so lucky as the neighbouring county of cornwall and have not the share of representative which the latter possesse where i have know a moderate country gentleman with a few score of hundred per annum from his estate treble his income by return three or four member to parliament and by the influence with ministers which these seat give he
the parliamentary interest of the house of lyndon have be grossly neglect during my wife s minority and the incapacity of the earl her father or to speak more correctly it have be smuggle away from the lyndon family altogether by the adroit old hypocrite of tiptoff castle who act as most kinsman and guardian do by their ward and relative and rob they
the marquess of tiptoff return four member to parliament two for the borough of tippleton which as all the world know lie at the foot of our estate of hackton bound on the other side by tiptoff park
for time out of mind we have send member for that borough until tiptoff take advantage of the late lord s imbecility put in his own nominee
when his eld son become of age of course my lord be to take his seat for tippleton when rigby nabob rigby who make his fortune under clive in india die the marquess thought fit to bring down his second son my lord george poynings to whom i have introduce the reader in a former chapter and determine in his high mightiness that he too should go in and swell the rank of the opposition--the big old whig with whom the marquess act
rigby have be for some time in an ailing condition previous to his demise and you may be sure that the circumstance of his fail health have not be pass over by the gentry of the county who be staunch government man for the most part and hat my lord tiptoff s principle as dangerous and ruinous we have be look out for a man to fight against he say the squire to i we can only match tiptoff out of hackton castle
you mr
lyndon be our man and at the next county election we will swear to bring you in i hat the tiptoffs so that i would have fight they at any election
they not only would not visit at hackton but decline to receive those who visit we they keep the woman of the county from receive my wife they invent half the wild story of my profligacy and extravagance with which the neighbourhood be entertain they say i have frighten my wife into marriage and that she be a lost woman they hint that bullingdon s life be not secure under my roof that his treatment be odious and that i want to put he out of the way to make place for bryan my son
i could scarce have a friend to hackton but they count the bottle drunk at my table
they ferret out my dealing with my lawyer and agent
if a creditor be unpaid every item of his bill be know at tiptoff hall if i look at a farmer s daughter it be say i have ruin she
my fault be many i confess and as a domestic character i cannot boast of any particular regularity or temper but lady lyndon and i do not quarrel more than fashionable people do and at first we always use to make it up pretty well
i be a man full of error certainly but not the devil that these odious backbiter at tiptoff represent i to be
for the first three year i never strike my wife but when i be in liquor
when i fling the carving-knife at bullingdon i be drunk as everybody present can testify but as for have any systematic scheme against the poor lad i can declare solemnly that beyond merely hat he and one s inclination be not in one s power i be guilty of no evil towards he
i have sufficient motif then for enmity against the tiptoffs and be not a man to let a feeling of that kind lie inactive
though a whig or perhaps because a whig the marquess be one of the haughty man breathe and treat commoner as his idol the great earl use to treat they--after he come to a coronet himself-- as so many low vassal who might be proud to lick his shoe-buckle
when the tippleton mayor and corporation wait upon he he receive they cover never offer mr
mayor a chair but retire when the refreshment be bring or have they serve to the worshipful aldermen in the steward s room
these honest britons never rebel against such treatment until instruct to do so by my patriotism
no the dog like to be bully and in the course of a long experience i have meet with but very few englishman who be not of their way of thinking
it be not until i open their eye that they know their degradation
i invite the mayor to hackton and mrs
mayoress a very buxom pretty groceress she be by the way i make sit by my wife and drive they both out to the race in my curricle
lady lyndon fight very hard against this condescension but i have a way with she as the saying be and though she have a temper yet i have a well one
a temper psha
a wild-cat have a temper but a keeper can get the well of it and i know very few woman in the world whom i could not master
well i make much of the mayor and corporation send they buck for their dinner or ask they to mine make a point of attend their assembly dance with their wife and daughter go through in short all the act of politeness which be necessary on such occasion and though old tiptoff must have see my going on yet his head be so much in the cloud that he never once condescend to imagine his dynasty could be overthrow in his own town of tippleton and issue his mandate as securely as if he have be the grand turk and the tippletonians no well than so many slave of his will
every post which bring we any account of rigby s increase illness be the sure occasion of a dinner from i so much so that my friend of the hunt use to laugh and say rigby s bad therebe a corporation dinner at hackton it be in 1776 when the american war break out that i come into parliament
my lord chatham whose wisdom his party in those day use to call superhuman raise his oracular voice in the house of peers against the american contest and my countryman mr
burke--a great philosopher but a plaguy long-wind orator--be the champion of the rebel in the commons--where however thank to british patriotism he could get very few to back he
old tiptoff would have swear black be white if the great earl have bid he and he make his son give up his commission in the guards in imitation of my lord pitt who resign his ensigncy rather than fight against what he call his american brother
but this be a height of patriotism extremely little relish in england where ever since the breaking out of hostility our people hat the americans heartily and where when we hear of the fight of lexington and the glorious victory of bunker s hill as we use to call it in those day the nation flush out in its usual hot-headed anger
the talk be all against the philosopher after that and the people be most indomitably loyal
it be not until the land-tax be increase that the gentry begin to grumble a little but still my party in the west be very strong against the tiptoffs and i determine to take the field and win as usual
the old marquess neglect every one of the decent precaution which be requisite in a parliamentary campaign
he signify to the corporation and freeholder his intention of present his son lord george and his desire that the latter should be elect their burgess but he scarcely give so much as a glass of beer to whet the devotedness of his adherent and i as i need not say engage every tavern in tippleton in my behalf
there be no need to go over the twenty-time-tell tale of an election
i rescue the borough of tippleton from the hand of lord tiptoff and his son lord george
i have a savage sort of satisfaction too in force my wife who have be at one time exceedingly smite by her kinsman as i have already relate to take part against he and to wear and distribute my colour when the day of election come
and when we speak at one another i tell the crowd that i have beat lord george in love that i have beat he in war and that i would now beat he in parliament and so i do as the event prove for to the inexpressible anger of the old marquess barry lyndon esquire be return member of parliament for tippleton in place of john rigby esquire deceased and i threaten he at the next election to turn he out of both his seat and go to attend my duty in parliament
it be then i seriously determine on achieve for myself the irish peerage to be enjoy after i by my beloved son and heir
chapter xviii my good fortune begin to waver and now if any people should be dispose to think my history immoral for i have hear some assert that i be a man who never deserve that so much prosperity should fall to my share i will beg those caviller to do i the favour to read the conclusion of my adventure when they will see it be no such great prize that i have win and that wealth splendour thirty thousand per annum and a seat in parliament be often purchase at too dear a rate when one have to buy those enjoyment at the price of personal liberty and saddle with the charge of a troublesome wife
they be the deuce these troublesome wife and that be the truth
no man know until he try how wearisome and dishearten the burthen of one of they be and how the annoyance grow and strengthen from year to year and the courage become weak to bear it so that that trouble which seem light and trivial the first year become intolerable ten year after
i have hear of one of the classical fellow in the dictionary who begin by carry a calf up a hill every day and so continue until the animal grow to be a bull which he still easily accommodate upon his shoulder but take my word for it young unmarried gentleman a wife be a very much hard pack to the back than the big heifer in smithfield and if i can prevent one of you from marry the memoirs of barry lyndon esq will not be write in vain
not that my lady be a scold or a shrew as some wife be i could have manage to have cure she of that but she be of a cowardly cry melancholy maudlin temper which be to i still more odious do what one would to please she she would never be happy or in good- humour
i leave she alone after a while and because as be natural in my case where a disagreeable home oblige i to seek amusement and companion abroad she add a mean detestable jealousy to all her other fault i could not for some time pay the commonest attention to any other woman but my lady lyndon must weep and wring her hand and threaten to commit suicide and i know not what
her death would have be no comfort to i as i leave any person of common prudence to imagine for that scoundrel of a young bullingdon who be now grow up a tall gawky swarthy lad and about to become my great plague and annoyance would have inherit every penny of the property and i should have be leave considerably poorer even than when i marry the widow for i spend my personal fortune as well as the lady s income in the keeping up of our rank and be always too much a man of honour and spirit to save a penny of lady lyndon s income
let this be fling in the tooth of my detractor who say i never could have so injure the lyndon property have i not be make a private purse for myself and who believe that even in my present painful situation i have hoard of gold lay by somewhere and could come out as a croesus when i choose
i never raise a shilling upon lady lyndon s property but i spend it like a man of honour besides incur numberless personal obligation for money which all go to the common stock
independent of the lyndon mortgage and incumbrance i owe myself at least one hundred and twenty thousand pound which i spend while in occupancy of my wife s estate so that i may justly say that property be indebted to i in the above-mention sum
although i have describe the utter disgust and distaste which speedily take possession of my breast as regard lady lyndon and although i take no particular pain for i be all frankness and above-board to disguise my feeling in general yet she be of such a mean spirit that she pursue i with her regard in spite of my indifference to she and would kindle up at the small kind word i speak to she
the fact be between my respected reader and myself that i be one of the handsome and most dashing young man of england in those day and my wife be violently in love with i and though i say it who shouldnot as the phrase go my wife be not the only woman of rank in london who have a favourable opinion of the humble irish adventurer
what a riddle these woman be i have often think
i have see the most elegant creature at st
james s grow wild for love of the coarse and most vulgar of man the clever woman passionately admire the most illiterate of our sex and so on
there be no end to the contrariety in the foolish creature and though i donot mean to hint that i be vulgar or illiterate as the person mention above i would cut the throat of any man who dare to whisper a word against my birth or my breeding yet i have show that lady lyndon have plenty of reason to dislike i if she choose but like the rest of her silly sex she be govern by infatuation not reason and up to the very last day of our be together would be reconcile to i and fondle i if i address she a single kind word
ah she would say in these moment of tenderness-- ah redmond if you would always be so and in these fit of love she be the most easy creature in the world to be persuade and would have sign away her whole property have it be possible
and i must confess it be with very little attention on my part that i could bring she into good-humour
to walk with she on the mall or at ranelagh to attend she to church at st
james s to purchase any little present or trinket for she be enough to coax she
such be female inconsistency
the next day she would be call i mr
barry probably and be bemoan her miserable fate that she ever should have be unite to such a monster
so it be she be please to call one of the most brilliant man in his majesty s three kingdom and i warrant i other lady have a much more flattering opinion of i
then she would threaten to leave i but i have a hold of she in the person of her son of whom she be passionately fond i donot know why for she have always neglect bullingdon her old son and never bestow a thought upon his health his welfare or his education
it be our young boy then who form the great bond of union between i and her ladyship and there be no plan of ambition i could propose in which she would not join for the poor lad s behoof and no expense she would not eagerly incur if it might by any mean be show to tend to his advancement
i can tell you bribe be administer and in high place too so near the royal person of his majesty that you would be astonish be i to mention what great personage condescend to receive our loan
i get from the english and irish herald a description and detailed pedigree of the barony of barryogue and claim respectfully to be reinstate in my ancestral title and also to be reward with the viscounty of ballybarry
this head would become a coronet my lady would sometimes say in her fond moment smooth down my hair and indeed there be many a puny whipster in their lordships house who have neither my presence nor my courage my pedigree nor any of my merit
the striving after this peerage i consider to have be one of the most unlucky of all my unlucky dealing at this period
i make unheard-of sacrifice to bring it about
i lavish money here and diamond there
i buy land at ten time their value purchase picture and article of vertu at ruinous price
i give repeat entertainment to those friend to my claim who be about the royal person be likely to advance it
i lose many a bet to the royal dukes his majesty s brother but let these matter be forget and because of my private injury let i not be deficient in loyalty to my sovereign
the only person in this transaction whom i shall mention openly be that old scamp and swindler gustavus adolphus thirteenth earl of crabs
this nobleman be one of the gentleman of his majesty s closet and one with whom the revered monarch be on term of considerable intimacy
a close regard have spring up between they in the old king s time when his royal highness play at battledore and shuttlecock with the young lord on the landing-place of the great staircase at kew in some moment of irritation the prince of wales kick the young earl downstairs who fall break his leg
the prince s hearty repentance for his violence cause he to ally himself closely with the person whom he have injure and when his majesty come to the throne there be no man it be say of whom the earl of bute be so jealous as of my lord crabs
the latter be poor and extravagant and bute get he out of the way by send he on the russian and other embassy but on this favourite s dismissal crabs speed back from the continent and be appoint almost immediately to a place about his majesty s person
it be with this disreputable nobleman that i contract an unluckly intimacy when fresh and unsuspecting i first establish myself in town after my marriage with lady lyndon and as crabs be really one of the most entertaining fellow in the world i take a sincere pleasure in his company besides the interesting desire i have in cultivate the society of a man who be so near the person of the high personage in the realm
to hear the fellow you would fancy that there be scarce any appointment make in which he have not a share
he tell i for instance of charles fox be turn out of his place a day before poor charley himself be aware of the fact
he tell i when the howes be come back from america and who be to succeed to the command there
not to multiply instance it be upon this person that i fix my chief reliance for the advancement of my claim to the barony of barryogue and the viscounty which i propose to get
one of the main cause of expense which this ambition of mine entail upon i be the fitting out and arm a company of infantry from the castle lyndon and hackton estate in ireland which i offer to my gracious sovereign for the campaign against the american rebel
these troop superbly equip and clothe be embark at portsmouth in the year 1778 and the patriotism of the gentleman who have raise they be so acceptable at court that on be present by my lord north his majesty condescend to notice i particularly and say thatbe right mr
lyndon raise another company and go with they too but this be by no mean as the reader may suppose to my notion
a man with thirty thousand pound per annum be a fool to risk his life like a common beggar and on this account i have always admire the conduct of my friend jack bolter who have be a most active and resolute cornet of horse and as such engage in every scrape and skirmish which could fall to his lot but just before the battle of minden he receive news that his uncle the great army contractor be dead and have leave he five thousand per annum
jack that instant apply for leave and as it be refuse he on the eve of a general action my gentleman take it and never fire a pistol again except against an officer who question his courage and whom he wing in such a cool and determined manner as show all the world that it be from prudence and a desire of enjoy his money not from cowardice that he quit the profession of arm
when this hackton company be raise my stepson who be now sixteen year of age be most eager to be allow to join it and i would have gladly consent to have be rid of the young man but his guardian lord tiptoff who thwart i in everything refuse his permission and the lad s military inclination be balk
if he could have go on the expedition and a rebel rifle have put an end to he i believe to tell the truth i should not have be grieve over-much and i should have have the pleasure of see my other son the heir to the estate which his father have win with so much pain
the education of this young nobleman have be i confess some of the loose and perhaps the truth be i did neglect the brat
he be of so wild savage and insubordinate a nature that i never have the least regard for he and before i and his mother at least be so moody and dull that i think instruction throw away upon he and leave he for the most part to shift for himself
for two whole year he remain in ireland away from we and when in england we keep he mainly at hackton never care to have the uncouth ungainly lad in the genteel company in the capital in which we naturally mingle
my own poor boy on the contrary be the most polite and engage child ever see it be a pleasure to treat he with kindness and distinction and before he be five year old the little fellow be the pink of fashion beauty and good breeding
in fact he could not have be otherwise with the care both his parent bestow upon he and the attention that be lavish upon he in every way
when he be four year old i quarrel with the english nurse who have attend upon he and about whom my wife have be so jealous and procure for he a french gouvernante who have live with family of the first quality in paris and who of course must set my lady lyndon jealous too
under the care of this young woman my little rogue learn to chatter french most charmingly
it would have do your heart good to hear the dear rascal swear mort de ma vie
and to see he stamp his little foot and send the manant and canaille of the domestic to the trente mille diable
he be precocious in all thing at a very early age he would mimic everybody at five he would sit at table and drink his glass of champagne with the good of we and his nurse would teach he little french catch and the last parisian song of vade and collard pretty song they be too and would make such of his hearer as understand french burst with laughing and i promise you scandalise some of the old dowager who be admit into the society of his mamma not that there be many of they for i do not encourage the visit of what you call respectable people to lady lyndon
they be sad spoiler of sport tale-bearer envious narrow-minded people make mischief between man and wife
whenever any of these grave personage in hoop and high heel use to make their appearance at hackton or in berkeley square it be my chief pleasure to frighten they off and i would make my little bryan dance sing and play the diable a quatre and aid he myself so as to scare the old frump
i never shall forget the solemn remonstrance of our old square-toe of a rector at hackton who make one or two vain attempt to teach little bryan latin and with whose innumerable child i sometimes allow the boy to associate
they learn some of bryan s french song from he which their mother a poor soul who understand pickle and custard much well than french use fondly to encourage they in singing but which their father one day hearing he send miss sarah to her bedroom and bread and water for a week and solemnly horse master jacob in the presence of all his brother and sister and of bryan to whom he hop that flogging would act as a warning
but my little rogue kick and plunge at the old parson s shin until he be oblige to get his sexton to hold he down and swore corbleu morbleu ventrebleu that his young friend jacob should not be maltreat
after this scene his reverence forbid bryan the rectory-house on which i swear that his eld son who be bring up for the ministry should never have the succession of the living of hackton which i have thought of bestow on he and his father say with a cant hypocritical air which i hate that heaven s will must be do that he would not have his child disobedient or corrupt for the sake of a bishopric and write i a pompous and solemn letter charge with latin quotation take farewell of i and my house
i do so with regret add the old gentleman for i have receive so many kindness from the hackton family that it go to my heart to be disunite from they
my poor i fear may suffer in consequence of my separation from you and my be hence-forward unable to bring to your notice instance of distress and affliction which when they be know to you i will do you the justice to say your generosity be always prompt to relieve there may have be some truth in this for the old gentleman be perpetually pester i with petition and i know for a certainty from his own charity be often without a shilling in his pocket but i suspect the good dinner at hackton have a considerable share in cause his regret at the dissolution of our intimacy and i know that his wife be quite sorry to forego the acquaintance of bryan s gouvernante mademoiselle louison who have all the new french fashion at her finger end and who never go to the rectory but you would see the girl of the family turn out in new sack or mantle the sunday after
i use to punish the old rebel by snore very loud in my pew on sundays during sermon-time and i get a governor presently for bryan and a chaplain of my own when he become of age sufficient to be separate from the woman s society and guardianship
his english nurse i marry to my head gardener with a handsome portion his french gouvernante i bestow upon my faithful german fritz not forget the dowry in the latter instance and they set up a french dining-house in soho and i believe at the time i write they be rich in the world s good than their generous and free-handed master
for bryan i now get a young gentleman from oxford the rev
edmund lavender who be commission to teach he latin when the boy be in the humour and to ground he in history grammar and the other qualification of a gentleman
lavender be a precious addition to our society at hackton
he be the mean of make a deal of fun there
he be the butt of all our joke and bear they with the most admirable and martyrlike patience
he be one of that sort of man who would rather be kick by a great man than not be notice by he and i have often put his wig into the fire in the face of the company when he would laugh at the joke as well as any man there
it be a delight to put he on a high-mettle horse and send he after the hounds pale sweating call on we for heaven s sake to stop and hold on for dear life by the mane and the crupper
how it happen that the fellow be never kill i know not but i suppose hang be the way in which his neck will be break
he never meet with any accident to speak of in our hunting-match but you be pretty sure to find he at dinner in his place at the bottom of the table make the punch whence he would be carry off fuddle to bed before the night be over
many a time have bryan and i paint his face black on those occasion
we put he into a haunted room and frighten his soul out of his body with ghost we let loose cargo of rat upon his bed we cry fire and fill his boot with water we cut the leg of his preaching-chair and fill his sermon-book with snuff
poor lavender bear it all with patience and at our party or when we come to london be amply repay by be allow to sit with the gentlefolk and to fancy himself in the society of man of fashion
it be good to hear the contempt with which he talk about our rector
he have a son sir who be a servitor and a servitor at a small college he would say
how could you my dear sir think of give the reversion of hackton to such a low-breed creature i should now speak of my other son at least my lady lyndon s i mean the viscount bullingdon
i keep he in ireland for some year under the guardianship of my mother whom i have instal at castle lyndon and great i promise you be her state in that occupation and prodigious the good soul s splendour and haughty bearing
with all her oddity the castle lyndon estate be the well manage of all our possession the rent be excellently pay the charge of get they in small than they would have be under the management of any steward
it be astonish what small expense the good widow incur although she keep up the dignity of the two family as she would say
she have a set of domestic to attend upon the young lord she never go out herself but in an old gilt coach and six the house be keep clean and tight the furniture and garden in the good repair and in our occasional visit to ireland we never find any house we visit in such good condition as our own
there be a score of ready serve-lass and half as many trim man about the castle and everything in as fine condition as the good housekeeper could make it
all this she do with scarcely any charge to we for she feed sheep and cattle in the park and make a handsome profit of they at ballinasloe she supply i donot know how many town with butter and bacon and the fruit and vegetable from the garden of castle lyndon get the high price in dublin market
she have no waste in the kitchen as there use to be in most of our irish house and there be no consumption of liquor in the cellar for the old lady drank water and see little or no company
all her society be a couple of the girl of my ancient flame nora brady now mrs
quin who with she husband have spend almost all their property and who come to see i once in london look very old fat and slatternly with two dirty child at her side
she weep very much when she see i call i sir and mr
lyndon at which i be not sorry and beg i to help her husband which i do get he through my friend lord crabs a place in the excise in ireland and pay the passage of his family and himself to that country
i find he a dirty cast-down snivel drunkard and look at poor nora could not but wonder at the day when i have think she a divinity
but if ever i have have a regard for a woman i remain through life her constant friend and could mention a thousand such instance of my generous and faithful disposition
young bullingdon however be almost the only person with whom she be concern that my mother could not keep in order
the account she send i of he at first be such as give my paternal heart considerable pain
he reject all regularity and authority
he would absent himself for week from the house on sporting or other expedition
he be when at home silent and queer refuse to make my mother s game at piquet of evening but plunge into all sort of musty old book with which he muddle his brain more at ease laugh and chat with the piper and maid in the servant hall than with the gentry in the drawing-room always cut jibe and joke at mrs
barry at which she who be rather a slow woman at repartee would chafe violently in fact lead a life of insubordination and scandal
and to crown all the young scapegrace take to frequent the society of the romish priest of the parish-- a threadbare rogue from some popish seminary in france or spain-- rather than the company of the vicar of castle lyndon a gentleman of trinity who keep his hound and drank his two bottle a day
regard for the lad s religion make i not hesitate then how i should act towards he
if i have any principle which have guide i through life it have be respect for the establishment and a hearty scorn and abhorrence of all other form of belief
i therefore send my french body-servant in the year 17 to dublin with a commission to bring the young reprobate over and the report bring to i be that he have pass the whole of the last night of his stay in ireland with his popish friend at the mass-house that he and my mother have a violent quarrel on the very last day that on the contrary he kiss biddy and dosy her two niece who seem very sorry that he should go and that be press to go and visit the rector he absolutely refuse say he be a wicked old pharisee inside whose door he would never set his foot
the doctor write i a letter warn i against the deplorable error of this young imp of perdition as he call he and i could see that there be no love lose between they
but it appear that if not agreeable to the gentry of the country young bullingdon have a huge popularity among the common people
there be a regular crowd weep round the gate when his coach take its departure
score of the ignorant savage wretch run for mile along by the side of the chariot and some go even so far as to steal away before his departure and appear at the pigeon-house at dublin to bid he a last farewell
it be with considerable difficulty that some of these people could be keep from secrete themselves in the vessel and accompany their young lord to england
to do the young scoundrel justice when he come among we he be a manly noble-looking lad and everything in his bearing and appearance betoken the high blood from which he come
he be the very portrait of some of the dark cavalier of the lyndon race whose picture hang in the gallery at hackton where the lad be fond of spend the chief part of his time occupy with the musty old book which he take out of the library and which i hate to see a young man of spirit pore over
always in my company he preserve the most rigid silence and a haughty scornful demeanour which be so much the more disagreeable because there be nothing in his behaviour i could actually take hold of to find fault with although his whole conduct be insolent and supercilious to the high degree
his mother be very much agitated at receive he on his arrival if he feel any such agitation he certainly do not show it
he make she a very low and formal bow when he kiss her hand and when i hold out mine put both his hand behind his back star i full in the face and bent his head say mr
barry lyndon i believe turn on his heel and begin talk about the state of the weather to his mother whom he always style your ladyship she be angry at this pert bearing and when they be alone rebuke he sharply for not shake hand with his father
my father madam say he surely you mistake
my father be the right honourable sir charles lyndon
i at least have not forget he if other have it be a declaration of war to i as i see at once though i declare i be willing enough to have receive the boy well on his come amongst we and to have live with he on term of friendliness
but as man serve i i serve they
who can blame i for my after-quarrel with this young reprobate or lie upon my shoulder the evil which afterwards befell
perhaps i lose my temper and my subsequent treatment of he be hard
but it be he begin the quarrel and not i and the evil consequence which ensue be entirely of his creating
as it be good to nip vice in the bud and for a master of a family to exercise his authority in such a manner as that there may be no question about it i take the early opportunity of come to close quarter with master bullingdon and the day after his arrival among we upon his refusal to perform some duty which i request of he i have he convey to my study and thrash he soundly
this process i confess at first agitate i a good deal for i have never lay a whip on a lord before but i get speedily use to the practice and his back and my whip become so well acquainted that i warrant there be very little ceremony between we after a while
if i be to repeat all the instance of the insubordination and brutal conduct of young bullingdon i should weary the reader
his perseverance in resistance be i think even great than mine in correct he for a man be he ever so much resolve to do his duty as a parent cannot be flog his child all day or for every fault they commit and though i get the character of be so cruel a stepfather to he i pledge my word i spar he correction when he merit it many more time than i administer it
besides there be eight clear month in the year when he be quit of i during the time of my presence in london at my place in parliament and at the court of my sovereign
at this period i make no difficulty to allow he to profit by the latin and greek of the old rector who have christen he and have a considerable influence over the wayward lad
after a scene or a quarrel between we it be generally to the rectory-house that the young rebel would fly for refuge and counsel and i must own that the parson be a pretty just umpire between we in our dispute
once he lead the boy back to hackton by the hand and actually bring he into my presence although he have vow never to enter the door in my lifetime again and say he have bring his lordship to acknowledge his error and submit to any punishment i might think proper to inflict upon which i can he in the presence of two or three friend of mine with whom i be sit drink at the time and to do he justice he bear a pretty severe punishment without wince or cry in the least
this will show that i be not too severe in my treatment of the lad as i have the authority of the clergyman himself for inflict the correction which i think proper
twice or thrice lavender bryan s governor attempt to punish my lord bullingdon but i promise you the rogue be too strong for him and level the oxford man to the ground with a chair greatly to the delight of little byran who cry out bravo bully
thump he thump he and bully certainly do to the governor s heart s content who never attempt personal chastisement afterwards but content himself by bring the tale of his lordship s misdoing to i his natural protector and guardian
with the child bullingdon be strange to say pretty tractable
he take a liking for the little fellow as indeed everybody who see that darling boy did liked he the more he say because he be half a lyndon and well he might like he for many a time at the dear angel s intercession of papa donot flog bully to-day i have hold my hand and save he a horsing which he richly deserve
with his mother at first he would scarcely deign to have any communication
he say she be no long one of the family
why should he love she as she have never be a mother to he
but it will give the reader an idea of the dogged obstinacy and surliness of the lad s character when i mention one trait regard he
it have be make a matter of complaint against i that i deny he the education befit a gentleman and never send he to college or to school but the fact be it be of his own choice that he go to neither
he have the offer repeatedly from i who wish to see as little of his impudence as possible but he as repeatedly decline and for a long time i could not make out what be the charm which keep he in a house where he must have be far from comfortable
it come out however at last
there use to be very frequent dispute between my lady lyndon and myself in which sometimes she be wrong sometimes i be and which as neither of we have very angelical temper use to run very high
i be often in liquor and when in that condition what gentleman be master of himself
perhaps i do in this state use my lady rather roughly fle a glass or two at she and call she by a few name that be not complimentary
i may have threaten her life which it be obviously my interest not to take and have frighten she in a word considerably
after one of these dispute in which she run scream through the gallery and i as tipsy as a lord come staggering after it appear bullingdon be attract out of his room by the noise as i come up with she the audacious rascal trip up my heel which be not very steady and catch his fainting mother in his arm take she into his own room where he upon her entreaty swear he would never leave the house as long as she continue unite with i
i know nothing of the vow or indeed of the tipsy frolic which be the occasion of it i be take up glorious as the phrase be by my servant and put to bed and in the morning have no more recollection of what have occur any more than of what happen when i be a baby at the breast
lady lyndon tell i of the circumstance year after and i mention it here as it enable i to plead honourably not guilty to one of the absurd charge of cruelty trump up against i with respect to my stepson
let my detractor apologise if they dare for the conduct of a graceless ruffian who trip up the heel of his own natural guardian and stepfather after dinner
this circumstance serve to unite mother and son for a little but their character be too different
i believe she be too fond of i ever to allow he to be sincerely reconcile to she
as he grow up to be a man his hatred towards i assume an intensity quite wicked to think of and which i promise you i return with interest and it be at the age of sixteen i think that the impudent young hangdog on my return from parliament one summer and on my proposing to cane he as usual give i to understand that he would submit to no far chastisement from i and say grind his tooth that he would shoot i if i lay hand on he
i look at he he be grow in fact to be a tall young man and i give up that necessary part of his education
it be about this time that i raise the company which be to serve in america and my enemy in the country and since my victory over the tiptoff i scarce need say i have many of they begin to propagate the most shameful report regard my conduct to that precious young scapegrace my stepson and to insinuate that i actually wish to get rid of he
thus my loyalty to my sovereign be actually construe into a horrid unnatural attempt on my part on bullingdon s life and it be say that i have raise the american corp for the sole purpose of get the young viscount to command it and so of get rid of he
i be not sure that they have not fix upon the name of the very man in the company who be order to despatch he at the first general action and the bribe i be to give he for this delicate piece of service
but the truth be i be of opinion then and though the fulfilment of my prophecy have be delay yet i make no doubt it will be bring to pass ere long that my lord bullingdon need none of my aid in send he into the other world but have a happy knack of find the way thither himself which he would be sure to pursue
in truth he begin upon this way early of all the violent daring disobedient scapegrace that ever cause an affectionate parent pain he be certainly the most incorrigible there be no beating he or coax he or tame he
for instance with my little son when his governor bring he into the room as we be over the bottle after dinner my lord would begin his violent and undutiful sarcasm at i
dear child he would say begin to caress and fondle he what a pity it be i be not dead for thy sake
the lyndon would then have a worthy representative and enjoy all the benefit of the illustrious blood of the barrys of barryogue would they not mr
barry lyndon he always choose the day when company or the clergy or gentry of the neighbourhood be present to make these insolent speech to i
another day it be bryan s birthday we be give a grand ball and gala at hackton and it be time for my little bryan to make his appearance among we as he usually do in the smart little court- suit you ever see ah i
but it bring tear into my old eye now to think of the bright look of that darling little face
there be a great crowding and titter when the child come in lead by his half-brother who walk into the dancing-room would you believe it in his stocking-foot lead little bryan by the hand paddle about in the great shoe of the elder
donot you think he fit my shoe very well sir richard wargrave say the young reprobate upon which the company begin to look at each other and to titter and his mother come up to lord bullingdon with great dignity seize the child to her breast and say from the manner in which i love this child my lord you ought to know how i would have love his eld brother have he prove worthy of any mother s affection and burst into tear lady lyndon leave the apartment and the young lord rather discomfit for once
at last on one occasion his behaviour to i be so outrageous it be in the hunting-field and in a large public company that i lose all patience ride at the urchin straight wrench he out of his saddle with all my force and fling he roughly to the ground spring down to it myself and administer such a correction across the young caitiff s head and shoulder with my horsewhip as might have end in his death have i not be restrain in time for my passion be up and i be in a state to do murder or any other crime
the lad be take home and put to bed where he lie for a day or two in a fever as much from rage and vexation as from the chastisement i have give he and three day afterwards on send to inquire at his chamber whether he would join the family at table a note be find on his table and his bed be empty and cold
the young villain have flee and have the audacity to write in the follow term regard i to my wife his mother madam he say i have bear as long as mortal could endure the ill-treatment of the insolent irish upstart whom you have take to your bed
it be not only the lowness of his birth and the general brutality of his manner which disgust i and must make i hate he so long as i have the honour to bear the name of lyndon which he be unworthy of but the shameful nature of his conduct towards your ladyship his brutal and ungentlemanlike behaviour his open infidelity his habit of extravagance intoxication his shameless robbery and swindling of my property and your
it be these insult to you which shock and annoy i more than the ruffian s infamous conduct to myself
i would have stand by your ladyship as i promise but you seem to have take latterly your husband s part and as i cannot personally chastise this low-breed ruffian who to our shame be it speak be the husband of my mother and as i cannot bear to witness his treatment of you and loathe his horrible society as if it be the plague i be determined to quit my native country at least during his detest life or during my own
i possess a small income from my father of which i have no doubt mr
barry will cheat i if he can but which if your ladyship have some feeling of a mother leave you will perhaps award to i
messrs
childs the banker can have order to pay it to i when due if they receive no such order i shall be not in the least surprised know you to be in the hand of a villain who would not scruple to rob on the highway and shall try to find out some way in life for myself more honourable than that by which the penniless irish adventurer have arrive to turn i out of my right and home this mad epistle be sign bullingdon and all the neighbour vow that i have be privy to his flight and would profit by it though i declare on my honour my true and sincere desire after read the above infamous letter be to have the author within a good arm s length of i that i might let he know my opinion regard he
but there be no eradicating this idea from people s mind who insist that i want to kill bullingdon whereas murder as i have say be never one of my evil quality and even have i wish to injure my young enemy ever so much common prudence would have make my mind easy as i know he be go to ruin his own way
it be long before we hear of the fate of the audacious young truant but after some fifteen month have elapse i have the pleasure of be able to refute some of the murderous calumny which have be utter against i by produce a bill with bullingdon s own signature draw from general tarleton s army in america where my company be conduct itself with the great glory and with which my lord be serve as a volunteer
there be some of my kind friend who persist still in attribute all sort of wicked intention to i
lord tiptoff would never believe that i would pay any bill much more any bill of lord bullingdon s old lady betty grimsby his sister persist in declare the bill be a forgery and the poor dear lord dead until there come a letter to her ladyship from lord bullingdon himself who have be at new york at headquarters and who describe at length the splendid festival give by the officer of the garrison to our distinguished chieftain the two howes
in the meanwhile if i have murder my lord i could scarcely have be receive with more shameful obloquy and slander than now follow i in town and country
you will hear of the lad s death be sure exclaim one of my friend
and then his wife s will follow add another
he will marry jenny jones add a third and so on
lavender bring i the news of these scandal about i the country be up against i
the farmer on market-day use to touch their hat sulkily and get out of my way the gentleman who follow my hunt now suddenly secede from it and leave off my uniform at the county ball where i lead out lady susan capermore and take my place third in the dance after the duke and the marquis as be my willnot all the couple turn away as we come to they and we be leave to dance alone
sukey capermore have a love of dancing which would make her dance at a funeral if anybody ask she and i have too much spirit to give in at this signal instance of insult towards i so we dance with some of the very commonest low people at the bottom of the set--your apothecary wine-merchant attorney and such scum as be allow to attend our public assembly
the bishop my lady lyndon s relative neglect to invite we to the palace at the assize and in a word every indignity be put upon i which could by possibility be heap upon an innocent and honourable gentleman
my reception in london whither i now carry my wife and family be scarcely more cordial
on pay my respect to my sovereign at st
james s his majesty pointedly ask i when i have news of lord bullingdon
on which i reply with no ordinary presence of mind sir my lord bullingdon be fight the rebel against your majesty s crown in america
do your majesty desire that i should send another regiment to aid he on which the king turn on his heel and i make my bow out of the presence-chamber
when lady lyndon kiss the queen s hand at the drawing-room i find that precisely the same question have be put to her ladyship and she come home much agitated at the rebuke which have be administer to she
thus it be that my loyalty be reward and my sacrifice in favour of my country view
i take away my establishment abruptly to paris where i meet with a very different reception but my stay amidst the enchanting pleasure of that capital be extremely short for the french government which have be long tamper with the american rebel now openly acknowledge the independence of the united states
a declaration of war ensue all we happy english be order away from paris and i think i leave one or two fair lady there inconsolable
it be the only place where a gentleman can live as he like without be incommode by his wife
the countess and i during our stay scarcely see each other except upon public occasion at versailles or at the queen s play-table and our dear little bryan advance in a thousand elegant accomplishment which render he the delight of all who know he
i must not forget to mention here my last interview with my good uncle the chevalier de ballybarry whom i leave at brussels with strong intention of make his salut as the phrase be and who have go into retirement at a convent there
since then he have come into the world again much to his annoyance and repentance have fall desperately in love in his old age with a french actress who have do as most lady of her character do ruine he leave he and laugh at he
his repentance be very edifying
under the guidance of messieurs of the irish college he once more turn his thought towards religion and his only prayer to i when i see he and ask in what i could relieve he be to pay a handsome fee to the convent into which he propose to enter
this i could not of course do my religious principle forbid i to encourage superstition in any way and the old gentleman and i part rather coolly in consequence of my refusal as he say to make his old day comfortable
i be very poor at the time that be the fact and entre nous the rosemont of the french opera an indifferent dancer but a charming figure and ankle be ruin i in diamond equipage and furniture bill add to which i have a run of ill-luck at play and be force to meet my loss by the most shameful sacrifice to the money-lender by pawn part of lady lyndon s diamond that graceless little rosemont wheedle i out of some of they and by a thousand other scheme for raise money
but when honour be in the case be i ever find backward at her call and what man can say that barry lyndon lose a bet which he do not pay
as for my ambitious hope regard the irish peerage i begin on my return to find out that i have be lead wildly astray by that rascal lord crabs who like to take my money but have no more influence to get i a coronet than to procure for i the pope s tiara
the sovereign be not a whit more gracious to i on return from the continent than he have be before my departure and i have it from one of the aide-de-camp of the royal dukes his brother that my conduct and amusement at paris have be odiously misrepresent by some spy there and have form the subject of royal comment and that the king have influence by these calumny actually say i be the most disreputable man in the three kingdom
i disreputable
i a dishonour to my name and country
when i hear these falsehood i be in such a rage that i go off to lord north at once to remonstrate with the minister to insist upon be allow to appear before his majesty and clear myself of the imputation against i to point out my service to the government in vote with they and to ask when the reward that have be promise to i--viz the title hold by my ancestor--be again to be revive in my person
there be a sleepy coolness in that fat lord north which be the most provoking thing that the opposition have ever to encounter from he
he hear i with half-shut eye
when i have finish a long violent speech--which i make stride about his room in downing street and gesticulate with all the energy of an irishman--he open one eye smile and ask i gently if i have do
on my replying in the affirmative he say well mr
barry iwill answer you point by point
the king be exceedingly averse to make peer as you know
your claim as you call they have be lay before he and his majesty s gracious reply be that you be the most impudent man in his dominion and merit a halter rather than a coronet
as for withdraw your support from we you be perfectly welcome to carry yourself and your vote whithersoever you please
and now as i have a great deal of occupation perhaps you will do i the favour to retire so say he raise his hand lazily to the bell and bow i out ask blandly if there be any other thing in the world in which he could oblige i
i go home in a fury which cannot be describe and have lord crabs to dinner that day assail his lordship by pull his wig off his head and smother it in his face and by attack he in that part of the person where accord to report he have be formerly assault by majesty
the whole story be over the town the next day and picture of i be hang in the club and print- shop perform the operation allude to
all the town laugh at the picture of the lord and the irishman and i need not say recognise both
as for i i be one of the most celebrated character in london in those day my dress style and equipage be as well know as those of any leader of the fashion and my popularity if not great in the high quarter be at least considerable elsewhere
the people cheer i in the gordon row at the time they nearly kill my friend jemmy twitcher and burn lord mansfield s house down
indeed i be know as a staunch protestant and after my quarrel with lord north veer right round to the opposition and vex he with all the mean in my power
these be not unluckily very great for i be a bad speaker and the house would not listen to i and presently in 1780 after the gordon disturbance be dissolve when a general election take place
it come on i as all my mishap be in the habit of come at a most unlucky time
i be oblige to raise more money at most ruinous rate to face the confounded election and have the tiptoff against i in the field more active and virulent than ever
my blood boil even now when i think of the rascally conduct of my enemy in that scoundrelly election
i be hold up as the irish bluebeard and libel of i be print and gross caricature draw represent i flog lady lyndon whip lord bullingdon turn he out of door in a storm and i know not what
there be picture of a pauper cabin in ireland from which it be pretend i come other in which i be represent as a lacquey and shoeblack
a flood of calumny be let loose upon i in which any man of less spirit would have go down
but though i meet my accuser boldly though i lavish sum of money in the election though i fling open hackton hall and keep champagne and burgundy run there and at all my inn in the town as commonly as water the election go against i
the rascally gentry have all turn upon i and join the tiptoff faction it be even represent that i hold my wife by force and though i send she into the town alone wear my colour with bryan in her lap and make her visit the mayor s lady and the chief woman there nothing would persuade the people but that she live in fear and trembling of i and the brutal mob have the insolence to ask she why she dare to go back and how she like horsewhip for supper
i be throw out of my election and all the bill come down upon i together--all the bill i have be contract during the year of my marriage which the creditor with a rascally unanimity send in until they lie upon my table in heap
i willnot cite their amount it be frightful
my steward and lawyer make matter bad
i be bind up in an inextricable toil of bill and debt of mortgage and insurance and all the horrible evil attendant upon they
lawyer upon lawyer post down from london composition after composition be make and lady lyndon s income hamper almost irretrievably to satisfy these cormorant
to do her justice she behave with tolerable kindness at this season of trouble for whenever i want money i have to coax she and whenever i coax she i be sure of bring this weak and light-minded woman to good- humour who be of such a weak terrified nature that to secure an easy week with i she would sign away a thousand a year
and when my trouble begin at hackton and i determine on the only chance leave viz
to retire to ireland and retrench assign over the good part of my income to the creditor until their demand be meet my lady be quite cheerful at the idea of go and say if we would be quiet she have no doubt all would be well indeed be glad to undergo the comparative poverty in which we must now live for the sake of the retirement and the chance of domestic quiet which she hop to enjoy
we go off to bristol pretty suddenly leave the odious and ungrateful wretch at hackton to vilify we no doubt in our absence
my stud and hound be sell off immediately the harpy would have be glad to pounce upon my person but that be out of their power
i have raise by cleverness and management to the full as much on my mine and private estate as they be worth so the scoundrel be disappoint in this instance and as for the plate and property in the london house they could not touch that as it be the property of the heir of the house of lyndon
i pass over to ireland then and take up my abode at castle lyndon for a while all the world imagine that i be an utterly ruin man and that the famous and dash barry lyndon would never again appear in the circle of which he have be an ornament
but it be not so
in the midst of my perplexity fortune reserve a great consolation for i still
despatch come home from america announce lord cornwallis s defeat of general gates in carolina and the death of lord bullingdon who be present as a volunteer
for my own desire to possess a paltry irish title i care little
my son be now heir to an english earldom and i make he assume forthwith the title of lord viscount castle lyndon the third of the family title
my mother go almost mad with joy at salute she grandson as my lord and i feel that all my suffering and privation be repay by see this darling child advance to such a post of honour
chapter xix conclusion if the world be not compose of a race of ungrateful scoundrel who share your prosperity while it last and even when gorge with your venison and burgundy abuse the generous giver of the feast i be sure i merit a good name and a high reputation in ireland at least where my generosity be unbounded and the splendour of my mansion and entertainment unequalled by any other nobleman of my time
as long as my magnificence last all the country be free to partake of it i have hunter sufficient in my stable to mount a regiment of dragoon and butt of wine in my cellar which would have make whole county drunk for year
castle lyndon become the headquarters of score of needy gentleman and i never ride a- hunting but i have a dozen young fellow of the good blood of the country rid as my squire and gentleman of the horse
my son little castle lyndon be a prince his breeding and manner even at his early age show he to be worthy of the two noble family from whom he be descend i donot know what high hope i have for the boy and indulge in a thousand fond anticipation as to his future success and figure in the world
but stern fate have determine that i should leave none of my race behind i and ordain that i should finish my career as i see it close now-- poor lonely and childless
i may have have my fault but no man shall dare to say of i that i be not a good and tender father
i love that boy passionately perhaps with a blind partiality i deny he nothing
gladly gladly i swear would i have die that his premature doom might have be avert
i think there be not a day since i lose he but his bright face and beautiful smile look down on i out of heaven where he be and that my heart do not yearn towards he
that sweet child be take from i at the age of nine year when he be full of beauty and promise and so powerful be the hold his memory have of i that i have never be able to forget he his little spirit haunt i of night on my restless solitary pillow many a time in the wildest and maddest company as the bottle be go round and the song and laugh roar about i be think of he
i have get a lock of his soft brown hair hang round my breast now it will accompany i to the dishonour pauper s grave where soon no doubt barry lyndon s wear-out old bone will be lay
my bryan be a boy of amazing high spirit indeed how come from such a stock could he be otherwise impatient even of my control against which the dear little rogue would often rebel gallantly how much more then of his mother s and the woman s whose attempt to direct he he would laugh to scorn
even my own mother mrs
barry of lyndon the good soul now call herself in compliment to my new family be quite unable to check he and hence you may fancy what a will he have of his own
if it have not be for that he might have live to this day he might--but why repine
be he not in a well place
would the heritage of a beggar do any service to he
it be good as it be--heaven be good to us alas
that i his father should be leave to deplore he
it be in the month of october i have be to dublin in order to see a lawyer and a moneyed man who have come over to ireland to consult with i about some sale of mine and the cut of hackton timber of which as i hat the place and be greatly in want of money i be determined to cut down every stick
there have be some difficulty in the matter
it be say i have no right to touch the timber
the brute peasantry about the estate have be rouse to such a pitch of hatred against i that the rascal actually refuse to lay an axe to the tree and my agent that scoundrel larkins declare that his life be in danger among they if he attempt any further despoilment as they call it of the property
every article of the splendid furniture be sell by this time as i need not say and as for the plate i have take good care to bring it off to ireland where it now be in the good of keeping--my banker s who have advance six thousand pound on it which sum i soon have occasion for
i go to dublin then to meet the english man of business and so far succeed in persuade mr
splint a great shipbuilder and timber-dealer of plymouth of my claim to the hackton timber that he agree to purchase it off-hand at about one-third of its value and hand i over five thousand pound which be press with debt at the time i be fain to accept
he have no difficulty in get down the wood i warrant
he take a regiment of shipwright and sawyer from his own and the king s yard at plymouth and in two month hackton park be as bare of tree as the bog of allen
i have but ill luck with that accurse expedition and money
i lose the great part of it in two night play at daly s so that my debt stand just as they be before and before the vessel sail for holyhead which carry away my old sharper of a timber- merchant all that i have leave of the money he bring i be a couple of hundred pound with which i return home very disconsolately and very suddenly too for my dublin tradesman be hot upon i hear i have spend the loan and two of my wine- merchant have writ out against i for some thousand of pound
i buy in dublin accord to my promise however--for when i give a promise i will keep it at any sacrifice--a little horse for my dear little bryan which be to be a present for his tenth birthday that be now come on it be a beautiful little animal and stand i in a good sum
i never regard money for that dear child
but the horse be very wild
he kick off one of my horse- boy who ride he at first and break the lad s leg and though i take the animal in hand on the journey home it be only my weight and skill that make the brute quiet
when we get home i send the horse away with one of my groom to a farmer s house to break he thoroughly in and tell bryan who be all anxiety to see his little horse that he would arrive by his birthday when he should hunt he along with my hound and i promise myself no small pleasure in present the dear fellow to the field that day which i hop to see he lead some time or other in place of his fond father
ah i
never be that gallant boy to ride a fox-chase or to take the place amongst the gentry of his country which his birth and genius have point out for he
though i donot believe in dream and omen yet i cannot but own that when a great calamity be hang over a man he have frequently many strange and awful foreboding of it
i fancy now i have many
lady lyndon especially twice dream of her son s death but as she be now grow uncommonly nervous and vapourish i treat her fear with scorn and my own of course too
and in an unguarded moment over the bottle after dinner i tell poor bryan who be always question i about the little horse and when it be to come that it be arrive that it be in doolan s farm where mick the groom be break he in
promise i bryan scream his mother that you will not ride the horse except in company of your father but i only say pooh madam you be an ass be angry at her silly timidity which be always show itself in a thousand disagreeable way now and turn round to bryan say i promise your lordship a good flogging if you mount he without my leave i suppose the poor child do not care about pay this penalty for the pleasure he be to have or possibly think a fond father would remit the punishment altogether for the next morning when i rise rather late have sit up drink the night before i find the child have be off at daybreak have slipt through his tutor s room this be redmond quin our cousin whom i have take to live with i and i have no doubt but that he be go to doolan s farm
i take a great horsewhip and gallop off after he in a rage swear i would keep my promise
but heaven forgive i
i little thought of it when at three mile from home i meet a sad procession come towards i peasant moan and howl as our irish do the black horse lead by the hand and on a door that some of the folk carry my poor dear dear little boy
there he lie in his little boot and spur and his little coat of scarlet and gold
his dear face be quite white and he smile as he hold a hand out to i and say painfully you willnot whip i will you papa i could only burst out into tear in reply
i have see many and many a man dying and therebe a look about the eye which you cannot mistake
there be a little drummer-boy i be fond of who be hit down before my company at kuhnersdorf when i run up to give he some water he look exactly like my dear bryan then do--therebe no mistaking that awful look of the eye
we carry he home and scour the country round for doctor to come and look at his hurt
but what do a doctor avail in a contest with the grim invincible enemy
such as come could only confirm our despair by their account of the poor child s case
he have mount his horse gallantly sit he bravely all the time the animal plunge and kick and have overcome his first spite run he at a hedge by the roadside
but there be loose stone at the top and the horse s foot catch among they and he and his brave little rider roll over together at the other side
the people say they see the noble little boy spring up after his fall and run to catch the horse which have break away from he kick he on the back as it would seem as they lie on the ground
poor bryan run a few yard and then drop down as if shot
a pallor come over his face and they think he be dead
but they pour whisky down his mouth and the poor child revive still he could not move his spine be injure the low half of he be dead when they lay he in bed at home
the rest do not last long god help i
he remain yet for two day with we and a sad comfort it be to think he be in no pain
during this time the dear angel s temper seem quite to change he ask his mother and i pardon for any act of disobedience he have be guilty of towards we he say often he should like to see his brother bullingdon
bully be well than you papa he say he use not to swear so and he tell and teach i many good thing while you be away and take a hand of his mother and mine in each of his little clammy one he beg we not to quarrel so but love each other so that we might meet again in heaven where bully tell he quarrelsome people never go
his mother be very much affect by these admonition from the poor suffer angel s mouth and i be so too
i wish she have enable i to keep the counsel which the die boy give we
at last after two day he die
there he lay the hope of my family the pride of my manhood the link which have keep i and my lady lyndon together
oh redmond say she kneel by the sweet child s body do do let we listen to the truth out of his blessed mouth and do you amend your life and treat your poor love fond wife as her die child bade you and i say i would but there be promise which it be out of a man s power to keep especially with such a woman as she
but we draw together after that sad event and be for several month well friend
i willnot tell you with what splendour we bury he
of what avail be undertaker feather and herald trumpery
i go out and shoot the fatal black horse that have kill he at the door of the vault where we lay my boy
i be so wild that i could have shoot myself too
but for the crime it would have be well that i should perhaps for what have my life be since that sweet flower be take out of my bosom
a succession of misery wrong disaster and mental and bodily suffering which never fall to the lot of any other man in christendom
lady lyndon always vapourish and nervous after our bless boy s catastrophe become more agitated than ever and plunge into devotion with so much fervour that you would have fancy she almost distracted at time
she imagine she see vision
she say an angel from heaven have tell she that bryan s death be as a punishment to she for her neglect of her first-bear
